```
Am 31.08.2010 02:45, schrieb Bill Klein:
> As I have previously stated in this forum, when I used to work for/with
> vendors, I found a very funny (to me) distinction.  In the US, it was very
…[5 more quoted lines elided]…
> (but certainly NOT universal)

   Using SECTIONS makes a COBOL program look like a real programming 
language (ahem).

   One can be sure, that nobody can change the logic of the program by 
inserting a SECTION somewhere up or down the line, i.e. introduce a 
higher level structuring than the low level one (paragraph) one uses.

   My COBOL programs are structured into SECTIONs, and each SECTION has 
the same initial paragraph called ANFANG (German for "Begin"). The first 
section would be called HAUPT SECTION (haupt: GErman for "main").

   When I had to use COBOL'74, i.e. without proper structuring, sections 
might have had a final paragraph called ENDE (German for "end") with 
EXIT as the only instruction, so that an unavoidable GOTO (without COME 
FROM) could GOTO ENDE.

   Also the HAUPT (main) SECTION used to have an ENDE paragraph for the 
STOP PROGRAM or RETURN statement (which had to be in a paragraph by 
itself, I believe to remember correctly).

   All just a spell against spaghetti programming jumping back and forth 
thru the program code.


Cheers,
L.W.
```

- **From:** Lüko Willms <lueko.willms@domain.invalid>
- **Date:** 2010-09-04T14:47:59+02:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<4c823fc9$0$6274$6e1ede2f@read.cnntp.org>`
- **In-Reply-To:** `<4c82076f$0$6274$6e1ede2f@read.cnntp.org>`
- **References:** `<k_odo.95442$Bh.78468@en-nntp-12.dc1.easynews.com> <65c546c531f937e6034a80e58b433db1@msgid.frell.theremailer.net> <0d61d264-e722-4024-a58c-8cb40ca166b7@x25g2000yqj.googlegroups.com> <91c90e7e-694f-4f06-9480-32af25bc8413@q16g2000prf.googlegroups.com> <8e0d6dFtsnU1@mid.individual.net> <bkfn761nmlidgv2e1nck189v49opnffvrp@4ax.com> <fgReo.72008$MG3.66596@en-nntp-16.dc1.easynews.com> <6tho761749k7v09palqsmpe11f24enjgpo@4ax.com> <7hYeo.72691$Yn5.31587@en-nntp-14.dc1.easynews.com> <4c82076f$0$6274$6e1ede2f@read.cnntp.org>`

```
Am 04.09.2010 10:47, schrieb Lï¿½ko Willms:
>    My COBOL programs are structured into SECTIONs, and each SECTION has
> the same initial paragraph called ANFANG (German for "Begin"). The first
> section would be called HAUPT SECTION (haupt: GErman for "main").

>    Also the HAUPT (main) SECTION used to have an ENDE paragraph for the
> STOP PROGRAM or RETURN statement (which had to be in a paragraph by
> itself, I believe to remember correctly).

   After reading some more contributions to this thread, I want to add 
that, of course, PERFORM something THRU something-else is absolutely 
prohibited in the programs I am and have been responsible for.

   The above structuring makes also sure, that the program ends when its 
execution has reached the main section, and that there is no fall thru 
to other parts of the program, and other parts of the code can and will 
only be executed by a PERFORM this-or-that-section, and that thus a fall 
thru to other sections is excluded.

   Using the upper-most possible level of code structuring in COBOL 
(within the same source code unit), i.e. SECTION, assures the casual 
reader of the program, that those basic units of the program are not 
accidentally part of a larger SECTION, so that the code which she or or 
he thinks will be only executed as a single paragraph, cannot be 
executed as fall thru section of a PERFORM on the level of a SECTION.


Cheers,
L.W.
```

```
Howard Brazee wrote:
> On Tue, 31 Aug 2010 23:34:17 +1200, "Pete Dashwood"
> <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz> wrote:
…[8 more quoted lines elided]…
> documentation".   My memory won't do the job.

Funny you should say that. I had just such a query today. Not for 20 
year-old code but code that was in OO COBOL. It is a complex application and 
the first thing I did was reach for the delivery documentation I wrote at 
the time, and  retrieved the source code from an archive. The delivery 
document was aimed at a maintenance programmer and explained why certain 
approaches were taken and gave details that a programmer would need to know. 
Page 14 of it exlpained exctly what the guy was asking and page 18 of it had 
the solution to another problem that he had been trying to solve for some 
time. (it was ironic but I had actually written this in red, as it was so 
critical.)

His problem was not with understanding my code, it was with understanding 
the COBOL approach (he is not an experienced COBOL guy although he is an 
excellent programmer in other languages). I had been preparing to dive into 
the application and run his problem down but there was really no need. It 
was all in the documentation. He had read the release documentation when he 
took delivery but at that point, it just hadn't gelled. I always provide 
conceptual diagrams as well as documenting file and DB usage and interfaces 
and components. He apologised for overlooking the fact that what he needed 
to know was in the documentation, but I think it was understandable. It's 
only when you really get into something that the documentation makes sense.

I completely agree that memory cannot be relied on (especially as the years 
advance) but the detals of a complex system design are not as simple as the 
rules you apply when writing code. I don't have to remember every line of 
code I ever wrote to know that I only perform SECTIONs out of line, etc. It 
is so instinctive for me that any deviation would definitely be memorable. 
:-)

I should probably mention that my comments apply ONLY to code I have control 
over. I have worked on sites where they had different standards and I 
obviously complied with what they required. However, in developing my own 
code base (and sometimes on other sites too) I have been free to code as I 
see fit and the way I see fit is as previously described.

Pete.
```

   The problem is with COBOL -- there was no proper way of doing it in

weeknesses of COBOL always occupy a large part of the discussions among

Back in Doc's Golden Daze, I had occasion to modify a spaghetti
encoded Cobol program with rampant PERFORM ... THRU code structures.

and more economic than spending 6 weeks trying to understand the
convoluted knottage of code.

Am 26.08.2010 16:37, schrieb Fritz Wuehler:

   replying to Bill Klein's query:

>> I would be VERY interested in any current documentatin indicating that
>> >  the PERFORM statement (with any compiler) has "bad performance".

> The answer is to have a look at the code generated for PERFORM.

   The question actually was not, if such documentation warning against 
using PERFORM is true and well founded (for that compiler and system), 
but if there is such warning in the documentation provided by the 
compiler maker.

   To answer that, frankly, I haven't found any such warning in the at 
my time current COBOL compiler manuals of the three Sperry Univac 
systems I had worked on (on OS/1100 only COBOL'74, i.e. ACOB and 
COBOL'85 i.e. UCOB, not the obsolete "DOD COBOL").


Cheers,
L.W.
```

## Thread: OT: Support Documentation — 62 msgs

_Topics: [`Off-topic and spam`](topics.md#spam)_

### OT: Support Documentation

- **From:** Alistair <alistair@ld50macca.demon.co.uk>
- **Date:** 2010-09-01T04:37:37-07:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<77572f8d-5ae3-453e-a13b-fdc6c480653d@n3g2000yqb.googlegroups.com>`

```
Pete (NZ) touched on a subject that may be dear to the hearts of die-
hard Cobolers and has certainly been a bug-bear for me over the
years.

DOCUMENTATION.


Some years back I was involved in the development of a large batch
invoicing system. Subsequent to implementation, I was instrumental in
enhancing the system (adding in the bits which had had to be left out
of the original system development for reason of time) and supporting
the system in the live environment (bug fixes (not too many of those -
the major enhancement added only 8 post-implementation bugs) and minor
enhancements). The documentation for the system consisted of design
document (now woefully out of date and confusing), program specs, code
and JCL. No HOW-TO fixit notes....

I subsequently spent three months documenting the system in detail and
showing what ran when, what to do at any point where the system fell
over and also how to completely back out and rerun the system.
Unfortunately, the documentation quickly went out of date (as new
projects did not include elements to 'Update Documentation') and was
probably never used (the development project leader did not know the
file existed.

So.... what do you guys and gals consider to be the ideal
documentation for post-implementation support? For BATCH and ONLINE.
```

#### ↳ Re: OT: Support Documentation

- **From:** Ken <klshafer@att.net>
- **Date:** 2010-09-01T09:13:31-07:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<feaae93b-af64-42fd-a6d2-c368770930ac@l20g2000yqm.googlegroups.com>`
- **References:** `<77572f8d-5ae3-453e-a13b-fdc6c480653d@n3g2000yqb.googlegroups.com>`

```
On Sep 1, 7:37 am, Alistair <alist...@ld50macca.demon.co.uk> wrote:
> Pete (NZ) touched on a subject that may be dear to the hearts of die-
> hard Cobolers and has certainly been a bug-bear for me over the
…[23 more quoted lines elided]…
> documentation for post-implementation support? For BATCH and ONLINE.

In my shop where the code base is probably a couple of thousand batch
and online programs containing 2 to 3 million LOC, the prime
contractor did a fair job keeping the documentation up to date for the
first few years of maintenance. Then they let go the two documentation
people as "overhead" :-) and the documentation was updated on an
occasional basis, but at least in "one place" which always had the
latest info (bug descriptions, design docs, new tables added, etc.
etc.) about the programs.

Then another contractor took over and for the last six years the
"primary key" on all documentation is the "trouble report ID number",
and the documentation about the fixes was never consolidated in the
"one place" per program/subsystem.  The result is that if you want a
wholistic picture of "what is the design and business logic for the
XYZ system" you have to go hunting all over the place on a shared
network drive, for the business specs, design docs, testing results,
production implementation, and the like.

*** EXCEPT FOR *** (and this is the ta-da! part) -

The developers were fairly disciplined about putting comments for
Revision History with trouble-report-ID and inline comments identified
by trouble-report-ID. This means that the comments in the program code
are the SINGLE MOST COMPREHENSIVE documentation on post-implementation
support for that program. At least they provide a framework by which
other, more extensive searches, of MS-Word .doc documentation, may be
conducted by looking for references to trouble-report-ID.

As an aside, my organization is currently conducting a "documentation
clean up" project which is, as you can imagine, a massive effort. My
fear is that they will do it badly, and delete a bunch of stuff that
is important. My point of view is that since we currently do not have
a credibly comprehensive and documented and complete inventory of (1)
programs, and (2) DB tables, that they start with that. But I don't
think that is what they are doing.

Ken
```

#### ↳ Re: OT: Support Documentation

- **From:** Alistair <alistair@ld50macca.demon.co.uk>
- **Date:** 2010-09-09T05:07:30-07:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<71644481-473c-47dc-a6cd-85e8bbb62398@n7g2000vbo.googlegroups.com>`
- **References:** `<77572f8d-5ae3-453e-a13b-fdc6c480653d@n3g2000yqb.googlegroups.com>`

```
On Sep 1, 12:37 pm, Alistair <alist...@ld50macca.demon.co.uk> wrote:
> Pete (NZ) touched on a subject that may be dear to the hearts of die-


My thanks to Ken and Howard for their thoughts. Strange that something
which touches so heavily on IT doesn't attract more attention.

It is certainly true that the code represents the single best source
of documentation on what a program/system does but I have found
problems with it in the past:

1.  The code can be manifestly wrong with the in-stream documentation
as such showing what a program/system does rather than what it should
do.

2.  There is no simple definitive statement as to a program function
which can be quickly taken on board when fixing a problem at 3 a.m.
nor any clue as to what should be restored/deleted to accomplish a re-
run. This argument was used on at least one occasion to justify
avoiding nested IF conditions by the project leader.

3.  I have more than once come across in-place code which says: Remove
after (some date in the past). Should it be removed or left in situ?
Why was the comment and code left in place?


I have also come across the situation where documentation consisted of
original specifications and layers of specifications applying changes
without re-stating the original spec. Documentation like that can be a
nightmare especially if the contents don't match the cover (eg a spec
which has been cloned from another program but not updated correctly).


Finally, does anyone use UML for batch systems documentation?
```

##### ↳ ↳ Re: OT: Support Documentation

- **From:** "Pete Dashwood" <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz>
- **Date:** 2010-09-10T02:00:49+12:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<8es7kjFshkU1@mid.individual.net>`
- **References:** `<77572f8d-5ae3-453e-a13b-fdc6c480653d@n3g2000yqb.googlegroups.com> <71644481-473c-47dc-a6cd-85e8bbb62398@n7g2000vbo.googlegroups.com>`

```
Alistair wrote:
> On Sep 1, 12:37 pm, Alistair <alist...@ld50macca.demon.co.uk> wrote:
>> Pete (NZ) touched on a subject that may be dear to the hearts of die-
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
> which touches so heavily on IT doesn't attract more attention.

I didn't respond originally because in the modern world this is just not 
important.

Systems like Java, C++, VB.NET, and C# can all document themselves 
automatically, using the facilities of IDEs like Eclipse and Visual Studio.

In fact, even NetCOBOL for .NET (which uses Eclipse) has this facility.

Comments placed by the programmer can be organized automatically into useful 
documentation, along with comments generated by the IDE.

But most importantly, if you don't have thousands of lines of code, you can 
read it and see what it does pretty easily. In OO Languages, methods are 
encapsulated and usually easily assimilable. (At least, the ones I write 
are... :-)  Bad programmers will make life difficult whether you document or 
you don't...)


>
> It is certainly true that the code represents the single best source
…[5 more quoted lines elided]…
> do.

Modern tools can pick this up very quickly. You can generate a graphic 
representation of the logic and generate test data to try it with, 
automatically.

>
> 2.  There is no simple definitive statement as to a program function
> which can be quickly taken on board when fixing a problem at 3 a.m.

I see this 2/3 a.m. thing recurring frequently in this forum and I remember 
the days when it was necessary. Is anybody STILL getting out of bed to go 
and fix commercial software in the mddle of the night? If they are it is 
because an overnight batch run failed and it HAS to be ready by morning. 
Right?

I have a few thoughts on that:

1. Why is critical processing being run in a batch window? Why wasn't it 
sorted during the day when staff were there to fix it?

2. Why is a critical batch program running in production with bugs in it 
that cause it to fail?

3. If it REALLY has to be ready by tomorrow, why wasn't it run yesterday? 
OK, you had to wait for a particular cut-off date before you could run it. 
That is what happened in the 1950s. You are talking Collators and Calcs, not 
computers. We have online processing available and have had for a number of 
decades. The "critical" updates could have been done as they happened; the 
processing capability is there. Batch processing in 2010, simply reflects 
limited imagination in system design. The days when there had to be a 
printer online are long gone. Online processing can be writing report 
lines/invoices/sales report lines/whatever to a reporting repository or 
spool as the transactions occur. The job of collating them and printing them 
can be done offline. It doesn't need batch processing programs. In the Olden 
Dayze there wasn't the storage capacity or processing power to do this, so 
transactions could only be "partially completed" in real time and needed to 
do the grunt work  (usually file updating) in an overnight batch window, 
(where "transaction files" were applied against "master files").  Nowadays 
the "transaction file' is not needed and the updates can be applied by an 
online transaction, and backed out if it fails.

Just supposing it was IMPOSSIBLE for the problem to be corrected by 
tomorrow? Would the company fail?  No? Then why drag a programmer out of bed 
at 2:00 am to come and slap a band aid onto a program which obviously needs 
much more comprehensive analysis and repair? It is a recipe for disaster. 
Chances are the 2:00am fix is going to spawn a bunch of other 2:00am 
fixes... Managers who do this kind of "cope" management should be fired, in 
my opinion. It ISN'T "management"; it is "scraping through by the skin of 
our teeth". Well run installations don't have this problem. Code is properly 
developed, tested and implemented. Sure, there can still be the odd bug in 
production, but it NEVER necessitates getting someone who is half asleep to 
work on a critical company resource, and any manager who thinks that is OK 
is so far off the beam they shouldn't be managing.

Getting back to reality for a moment, when was the last time ANYBODY in this 
forum had to get up and go into work at 3:00am? For me it was over 30 years 
ago. Can we stop using it as a reason for documenting code please? Can we 
also stop using it as an excuse for dumbing down code instead of training 
people to write it properly?

There are enough good reasons for doing these things, without having to 
fabricate bad ones.

4. Even if the code is properly documented as you suggest, nobody's going to 
read it when they're half asleep at 3:00 am. Find the problem, fix the code 
or JCL, get back to bed...



> nor any clue as to what should be restored/deleted to accomplish a re-
> run.

OK, here you may have something. However, I'd expect that to be in the JCL 
not the program.


>This argument was used on at least one occasion to justify
> avoiding nested IF conditions by the project leader.

Yeah. I spose he wrote specs in non-joined-up writing using a crayon?

>
> 3.  I have more than once come across in-place code which says: Remove
> after (some date in the past). Should it be removed or left in situ?
> Why was the comment and code left in place?

Take it out and see what happens...

Can you not see how ridiculous this is getting?  If production programs 
(which never should have been written in the first place, if properly 
trained Analysts were employed) are failing in the middle of the night, what 
person in their right mind would suggest patching them while half asleep and 
under pressure?

They need proper analysis and investigation, in the light of day, by people 
who are competent and awake. Better still, the online system needs upgrading 
and these archaic batch programs should be discarded.

>
>
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
> which has been cloned from another program but not updated correctly).

And that is why most programmers don't trust documentation. It DOESN'T get 
updated (unless it is done automatically by software).

>
>
> Finally, does anyone use UML for batch systems documentation?

I did on a couple of projects but I found that it was more trouble than it 
was worth. Oops, sorry, I just saw you said for batch. No, I haven't used it 
for that, but I haven't defined a system that used batch for around 15 
years. (And I don't expect to ever again...) Components have replaced batch 
in my world.

BOTTOM LINE:

1. It is good to document code and it is good to do it hierarchically. 
Overview, then drill down.

2. It is good to comment program code, especially modules or components. 
Adding lines that just repeat what the code is doing (in English) is a 
pointless waste of time. (Unless you are on a contract where payment is 
based on LOC... anyone stupid enough to go for that deserves whatever they 
get...)

3. If you have an IDE that supports automatic documentation, it is good to 
use it.

4. There is no documentation you can write that will help someone who is 
half asleep. You'll be lucky if they read it when they are awake.

5. NO documentation you can write can ever be critical or vital, no matter 
how much you may want it to be. The ONLY thing that IS critical or vital is 
the program code.

You may recall that your post was prompted by an incident I described where 
a critical condition had been documented IN RED on the page. The person who 
missed it, thereby causing himself several days of aggravation, was not an 
idiot. He is a very capable and useful programmer who has been doing it for 
years. But he was working long hours and was under pressure. I spent days 
writing and illustrating what I hoped would be a useful document. By and 
large, it is, but when it was really needed, it made no difference.

I rest my case.

Pete.
```

If it is run during the day it does not include business-to-close-of-day 
data; such things are important when dealing with minor matters like stock 
trades, banking payments and other items which are date-specific.  

(If a contract states 'BigCorp can raise your credit card interest if it 
does not receive payment by the 23rd of the month' then BigCorp opens 
it'sself up to interesting legal actions if it does not process *all* 
payments received by 23:59:59.00 on the 22nd of the month... so BigCorp 
schedules the Apply Payments Received job to run some time after 00:00:00)

Legal niceties aside... I've worked in shops where off-peak fees were 
lower than peak-time fees; if a job is run 'during the day when staff were 
there' then it Costs More Money.

>that cause it to fail?

it later but never enough time to do it right, now'.  Bugs can manifest 
themselves at a variety of times and under a variety of conditions.

```
docdwarf@panix.com wrote:
> In article <8es7kjFshkU1@mid.individual.net>,
> Pete Dashwood <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz> wrote:
…[17 more quoted lines elided]…
> variant of 'That Costs Money!'

OK. Why would you work for such a company?


>
>>
…[20 more quoted lines elided]…
> it... only fair, wouldn't you say?'

Absolutely.But why would you work for such a company?
>
>> If they are it is
…[14 more quoted lines elided]…
> items which are date-specific.

Having worked for the London Stock Exchange (and a major European Money 
Market Brokerage) and a string of household name Banks (2 of which are 
American) I  know very well what you are saying. However all of these 
applications can (and actually are in a number of places) be handled by 
transactions with effective date/times in them. The process timestamp for a 
transaction does not have to coincide with the real time. The last 
transaction for the day can run at the last trading moment of the day or it 
could have been run several hours earlier and waiting activation.
In certain critical situations, transactions must be actioned as they 
happen, but that certainly isn't done in an overnight batch window. We can 
agree that some things are time critical; the issue is whether they need 
overnight batch processing or not. I contenbd that they don't. I further 
contend that if they do where you are working it is because of history, and 
not because of necessity. But from what you are describing of where you are 
working it seems unlikely they would modernise their systems (it would cost 
money) and you have a comfortable nook helping them maintain the old ones so 
it may be that my points are not applicable to that particular organization. 
Eventually, it will come to the point where they realize that what they ARE 
doing is costing them money (far more in the scheme of things than it would 
cost to buy some proper tools or invest in some proper training...), and 
they will HAVE to do something, but you will probably be retired by then... 
:-)



>
> (If a contract states 'BigCorp can raise your credit card interest if
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
> after 00:00:00)

Only if it is batch. You are assuming that is the only solution, and it 
isn't. Payments could be applied in real time as they are received
by Big Corp. They would be updated instantly and anything arriving with an 
effective datetime after 23:59:59:99on the 22nd would be late. Clients 
wishing to pay at the last minute would pay early, but with an effective 
datetime of 23:59:59:99 on the 22nd. Big Corp's system would show the funds 
pending much as an uncleared cheque is shown on your bank account. There are 
dozens of ways of handling such situations and still complying with legal 
and moral requirements. Batch processing is only one such way and it is 
anachronistic in this day and age.

>
> Legal niceties aside... I've worked in shops where off-peak fees were
> lower than peak-time fees; if a job is run 'during the day when staff
> were there' then it Costs More Money.

Then our experience differs. I have never worked anywhere when the rates for 
being called out in the middle of the night were LESS than the rates paid 
for working during the day (unless a shift rota was in operation, when the 
rates would be the same. But then the whole argument is moot. If you are 
officially supposed ot be on deck at 3:00am that is a different situation.
>
>> 2. Why is a critical batch program running in production with bugs
…[5 more quoted lines elided]…
> conditions.

Yes, Doc, I know. :-) Nevertheless, it seems that SOME shops have a lot more 
of these than others. And a correlation might be established between the 
acceptance of the need to invest in their team and their tools, and the 
incidence of critical programs failing at inconvenient times...
>
>>
…[15 more quoted lines elided]…
> and contracts.

No, your viewpoint is coloured by your experience, just as mine is. I am 
(and was) aware of time constraints in some areas of business, having worked 
in such businesses. I'm also very well aware (perhaps even more than 
yourself) of the varying legal requirements required by various 
jurisdictions around the world. (I once had to implement a sensitive 
contractual system into 14 European countries, all of which interpreted the 
contract law differently. I don't honestly think my imagination (or 
experience) is limited.) The issue is not that there are contractual, legal, 
and time constraints involved in certain types of data processing; the issue 
is that batch processing is the only solution. I don't believe it is.

It was once, but that was a consequence of storage and processing 
limitations, allied with the fact that system design tended to reflect what 
had been done manually and it was normal accounting practise to have 
cut-offs and specific "processing periods". With today's technology it is 
possible to approach these things differently. I can envision a time when 
there will be no batch processing; everything will happen instantly. Reports 
for "the last month" or "the last 4 days" or any period you like, will be 
instantly available from database subsystems that can produce such result 
sets in milliseconds. (In fact, my local bank here in NZ already offers 
that. I haven't had a paper statement for about 5 years, and I haven't 
written a cheque for 10. I can go online and immediately transfer funds to 
pay bills (if they are not paid automatically) and I can get a "statement" 
of my account for any time period I like up to two years in the past.

It isn't done by batch processing.

>
> [snip]
…[5 more quoted lines elided]…

you want to run a business, doesn't it? :-)

On Sep 9, 8:17 pm, docdw...@panix.com () wrote:
> In article <8eslcnFj2...@mid.individual.net>,
>

Thanks everyone for the comments. I didn't intend starting a war of
batch versus oltp but seeing as I have...


The batch system that I was thinking of was a large weekly invoicing
system with 2 million product line sales. Hindsight could have had
much of the processing done in ad hoc cycles but it wasn't until the
system had grown from a six hour run to a 48 hour run before the
clients thought about doing anything to it. One recurring abend was
the program that validated the pricing applied to the product lines.
It was coded to onluy allow approx 1000 price errors through before
abending with a message for the users. I so loved ringing the clients
at 2 am for their early morning call (I still remember being barely
able to hide the glee in my voice when I called the client the first
time). They wanted a better solution but they never allocated time for
anyone to consider the problem and potential fixes. Instead we got a
'How much would it cost to....' followed by @How long is a piece of
string' (String theorists need not answer that one as they can not
currently prove their theories). Due to the manner in which they chose
to apply their pricing changes we frequently had to back out the
system (and often the follow on systems too) to apply price fixes and
reruns.

Since moving on from that job, I have encountered other batch systems
with similar bulks of data (including the Inland Revenue).

Kerry seems to have hit the right solution (first suggested by Gerry
Weinberg in the early 80s) - ditch documentation. It seems to be
something that would work well with the Code N Fix development method.

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: OT: Support Documentation

_(thread depth: 7)_

- **From:** "Pete Dashwood" <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz>
- **Date:** 2010-09-11T01:59:57+12:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<8euruuFk8U1@mid.individual.net>`
- **References:** `<77572f8d-5ae3-453e-a13b-fdc6c480653d@n3g2000yqb.googlegroups.com> <8es7kjFshkU1@mid.individual.net> <i6astc$i4n$1@reader1.panix.com> <8eslcnFj25U1@mid.individual.net> <i6bbrp$bci$1@reader1.panix.com> <bb32a29a-3a72-4972-ab06-899dba03e76a@m1g2000vbh.googlegroups.com>`

```
Alistair wrote:
> On Sep 9, 8:17 pm, docdw...@panix.com () wrote:
>> In article <8eslcnFj2...@mid.individual.net>,
…[30 more quoted lines elided]…
> something that would work well with the Code N Fix development method.

I odn't believe any minds are being changed so I see no point in pursuing 
this.

I'll summarize my position as follows and that's it from me:

1. You seem to be equating the fact that volumes are large, with the need 
for batch processing. Such is no longer the case. Very large volumes can be 
processed by networked systems very effectively. It is partly to do with the 
fact that the network provides multiple processors and parallel pathways. 
The received wisdom that has been trotted out here is simply historical and 
based on the way mainframes were used in the 50s and 60s.

 If you are used to a mainframe environment then the received wisdom is 
batch processing (and in that environment it may well be an effective 
solution, although, with the changing role of mainframes in the current 
century, even this is becoming less true)). However, if the problem is 
approached from the point of view that "we have a few million transactions 
going on in a specified timeframe (where that timeframe could be very small) 
and can apply any technolohgy we like to getting them processed" then there 
are many advantages in getting off batch solutions.

2. I don't accept that modern systems must wait on elapsed time in order to 
process transactions, many systems easily process and differentiate between 
current, deferred, and pending transactions.

3. I don't accept the 2:00 am argument as being a reason to document code; 
there are other much better reasons to do this. Neither would I accept this 
as a reason to dumb down code and limit the power of it.

4. I don't believe that any detailed documentation is of much use at all, 
and would prefer to see better (clearer, concise, and succinct) code being 
written.

5. I DO believe that graphic documentation is often better and more useful 
than wordy descriptions of processes.

And finally, I really don't care whether you document or you don't :-)

Pete.
```

On Sep 9, 1:55 pm, "Pete Dashwood"

> Sure. I understand :-)

Dear Mr. Pete, I know you're just *delightfully engaged* with the Doc,
so I can cut you an inordinate amount of slack :-), but assuming your
question is not entirely rhetorical, allow me to answer "why would *I*
work for such a company?"

There have been an abundance of reasons for me, including -

1. To be close to an aging or ailing relative.
2. Because I like the co-workers.
3. Because I like and support the _business end_ of the organization,
which I feel good about because it is socially constructive.
4. Because the _application_ (the "business" of the business) is just
plain interesting.
5. Because the pay is good.
6. Because it was in a City that I wanted to experience.
7. Because the scenery (wimmin) is good :-).

and probably what historically has been the most important or
frequently occurring reason for me,

8. Because I wasn't aware of these shortcomings when I signed on, but
because when I sign on I sign on to the FINISH THE JOB, and when I ask
myself, 'Can the job be FINISHED, and FINISHED SUCCESSFULLY despite
these shortcomings?', I get an Answer of 'Yes', so then I stick around
and FINISH the $#@!*^! job! Guess that's why I end up being a
'software cleaner' - with apologies to Harvey Keitel a la _Pulp
Fiction_ and _Point of No Return_ (my gawd, Brigette Fonda was such a
fox in that one...) :-)

But hey, that's just me. Maybe others have a much lower tolerance for
frustration. :-)

There are any number of reasons, and most of them are not related to
technical issues, that we find ourselves working where we do. And that
does not, and should not, deter us from trying to make the place a
little more bearable despite its shortcomings, rather than just
pulling up stakes in that quest for the 'nearly perfect development
environment'.

And that's what I'm hearing from Alistair.

No matter, Pete, I still can't write quite as well as you do. That's
OK. I can write gud enuf to get my point across :-).

Ken

Ken wrote:
> On Sep 9, 1:55 pm, "Pete Dashwood"
> <dashw...@removethis.enternet.co.nz> wrote:
…[62 more quoted lines elided]…
> Ken

Your point is very well made, Ken.

Having knocked around the IT scene for a number of years, I am well aware 
why people stay in jobs and much of what I wrote to Doc was rhetorical. 
(Although I do believe that people should look for another job if they are 
REALLY not happy with conditions.) It seems to me there are only three 
options:

1. Do something about the things that are wrong.
2. If you REALLY can't effect positive change, move on. This is not 
petulance; it is because working in a place that is not satisfying is 
represseing your own career (and personal) development.
3. If there are other un-work-related reasons why you need ot be there, then 
shut up and get on with it. You're not going anywhere and whingeing about it 
just increases the load for everybody.

Usually, it is the management that determine the corporate culture and if 
the management don't value the work force, then that is not a company you 
would really want to be working for. Definitely not long term.

Successful employment is symbiotic; they help you achieve your goals (and it 
isn't just about money) and you do the very best you can to see they stay in 
business so they can keep on helping you achieve your goals as well as 
theirs...

I have worked in corporations that were well managed with a positive culture 
where it was a pleasure to go to work, and I have worked in the other kind 
too.

There actually are very good companies around but it seems like we only hear 
about sick ones here.

Pete.

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: OT: Support Documentation

_(thread depth: 7)_

- **From:** Ken <klshafer@att.net>
- **Date:** 2010-09-11T12:47:13-07:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<5753364f-815d-4fde-925a-42a845aaa0db@t3g2000vbb.googlegroups.com>`
- **References:** `<77572f8d-5ae3-453e-a13b-fdc6c480653d@n3g2000yqb.googlegroups.com> <71644481-473c-47dc-a6cd-85e8bbb62398@n7g2000vbo.googlegroups.com> <8es7kjFshkU1@mid.individual.net> <i6astc$i4n$1@reader1.panix.com> <8eslcnFj25U1@mid.individual.net> <4ac71758-7401-416b-a315-e406c3112ced@q26g2000vbn.googlegroups.com> <8f00sqFrfeU1@mid.individual.net>`

```
On Sep 10, 8:30 pm, "Pete Dashwood"
<dashw...@removethis.enternet.co.nz> wrote:
> Ken wrote:
> > On Sep 9, 1:55 pm, "Pete Dashwood"
> > <dashw...@removethis.enternet.co.nz> wrote:
>
> Your point is very well made, Ken.

The point about Brigette Fonda, right? :-)

>
> Having knocked around the IT scene for a number of years, I am well aware
> why people stay in jobs and much of what I wrote to Doc was rhetorical.
> (Although I do believe that people should look for another job if they are
> REALLY not happy with conditions.)

Yes, there is a qualitative, not just quantitative, difference between
being just unhappy and being downright *miserable*.

> It seems to me there are only three
> options:
>
> 1. Do something about the things that are wrong.

Look for things that you can do that don't require management
approval, don't require management to spend money (Doc's Control Costs
Dictum), and don't violate management policy. Although all of these
make for some constraints on what you can do, there is still a fair
amount of freedom and wiggle room left, if you think creatively.

> 2. If you REALLY can't effect positive change, move on. This is not
> petulance; it is because working in a place that is not satisfying is
> represseing your own career (and personal) development.

If possible, it is almost always best to leave for "more money." And
make it real, so that you can say that to the Manager with utmost
sincerity and a truthful demeanor, for many Managers, though
technically not adept, have a built-in Lie Detector. It is kinda
weird, but in my experience nearly all Managers seemed to be more
money-motivated than most people. That kinda works to your advantage -
leaving for "more money" is something they almost universally
understand, and generally won't hold against you, and you can come
back later if things change there and you want to.

> 3. If there are other un-work-related reasons why you need ot be there, then
> shut up and get on with it. You're not going anywhere and whingeing about it
> just increases the load for everybody.

OH! There are still things you can do besides shut up!

First-level response is counting *your* money every week. Sometimes
that is all that is needed to get on with it.

Second-level response is "emotional distancing", or in cosmic-speak,
"transcendence". The uncharitable might call this "doesn't care." No
mind. I have been converted over to the school of thought which says
"passion in the workplace is highly overrated, and indifference is
insufficiently esteemed."

Third-level response is the most drastic. Fire your Manager, or at
least demote him. OK, you can't do this on the organization chart, or
the payroll, but you can do it where it counts - in your head and in
your Heart.

I was forty-five years old, with twenty-five years of experience,
before I realized I can do this.

Ahhh, yes, I still remember that Day of Enlightenment just like it was
yesterday. My Manager, who had abused me by strong-arming, cajoling,
coercing me into working 160 hours a month though I had told me the
precondition for my returning to his shop (we had prior experience
together) was that I could work only 130 a month because I had to
finish up a prior engagement with another client. (Like you and I
said, Pete, CLC'ers FINISH the PROJECT.) Well, his strong-arm tactics,
which I gave into because I was weak, resulted in some tardiness with
prior client, friction, and payment jeopardy, so by now I was already
pretty aggravated.

One day he calls me up on the phone and asked me to do some other
Ridiculous Thing which I can't remember and doesn't matter, and at the
end of the call, I hung up *click* ...

... and then I realized he was Just Another Voice on the Phone. OK,
maybe a little more important, because he _did_ sign my time sheet,
but beyond that, Just Another Voice on the Phone. And I thought about
all the other Voices on the Phone there, the Users, the Peers, other
managers that I had to work with although they did not *employ* me.
And I thought about how keeping all the other Voices happy was a
nearly full time job in and of itself, and that had benefits to the
Manager as well, cause it kept *them* happy and off his back, and so
there was still *plenty* of work to do, so I decided right then and
there, I'm gonna demote this here Manager but continue to work there.
And I did.

There's a difference between "pro forma" compliance on the job, and
"pull out all the stops and bust your ass" effort. Good Managers can
tell the difference, but rarely do you need to demote a Good
Manager :-). Bad Managers, though they have a Lie Detector, still have
trouble differentiating "pro forma" from "pull out all the stops."
That's probably one of the reasons they're Bad Managers. So, be
careful to NEVER be insubordinate, and always comply with policy and
directives, but on a *pro forma* basis. But beyone that, treat the
Manager about the same as the cleaning crew that picks up the place
after 6:00 o'clock, NO, treat him a little less than that, because the
janitor has been rather nice to you and bids you good evening. Rather,
if it helps, envision scraping off the bottom of you shoe a glob of
dog sh*t that looks like his face, because that is the stature that he
has with you. :-)

It took _two full years_ for a Manager to fire me (fail to renew)
after I had fired him in this way (yes, even the slow learners
eventually catch on), so I still was able to get a lot of work done
and help a lot of people in the meantime.

> Usually, it is the management that determine the corporate culture and if
> the management don't value the work force, then that is not a company you
> would really want to be working for. Definitely not long term.

Very true that the beliefs at the top permeate downward. Is it Doc who
said the Fish Rots from the Head?

>
> Successful employment is symbiotic; they help you achieve your goals (and it
> isn't just about money) and you do the very best you can to see they stay in
> business so they can keep on helping you achieve your goals as well as
> theirs...

See above. Constructive Goals can be achieved even after you have
fired your Manager. You can still help *plenty* of people, good
people, even after you have fired your Manager. And you should do your
best to do so.

>
> I have worked in corporations that were well managed with a positive culture
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
> about sick ones here.

Well, no need to mention the Bad Places, is there? To be fair, let me
mention some of the Good Places I have been -

 - World Book Encylopedia
 - Honeywell Commercial Controls
 - SOHIO Petroleum (yes, THAT Sohio, the one that became BP)
 - Eli Lilly
 - Deloitte, Haskins, and Sells
 - Ford Motor (probably the best of all)

Ken
```

```
On Sat, 11 Sep 2010 12:47:13 -0700 (PDT), Ken <klshafer@att.net>
wrote:

```
In article <c659d415-0186-4328-843b-3ae6c3d063f9@e20g2000vbn.googlegroups.com>,
Alistair  <alistair@ld50macca.demon.co.uk> wrote:
>On Sep 11, 8:47?pm, Ken <klsha...@att.net> wrote:
>> On Sep 10, 8:30?pm, "Pete Dashwood"
…[21 more quoted lines elided]…
>Where does having a headache by 11 am fit in that?

As with any physical manifestation, Mr Maclean, it might be best to first 
examine physical causes.  When was the last time you had your vision 
checked?  Perhaps there is a blood-sugar cycle for which a bit of candy 
might be of use or a poorly placed/colored light which could be tended to.

Try just stepping out of the building and focussing your eyes on the 
horizon... human being aren't 'designed' to sit and stare at a screen 
18 - 24 inches away for hours on end.  Perhaps your posture might need 
some attention or you might want to spring a few bucks of your own money 
(you are *not* Management... *you* are willing to Spend Money to make 
things better) on a better chair.  (If co-workers give the ''Oooohh, isn't 
*he* the fancy one' trade an off-duty NIH physician a few cephalopods... 
errrr, squids... errrr, quid and have him write a prescription for 'better 
seating accomodations' or the like).  If the manifestations of discomfort 
are regular then examine the regular activity from a physical standpoint, 
not a working one.

DD

Like most posters here, I have never quit a contract or walked off a job, no 
matter how bad it gets, but I have come close on a few occasions.

I also did hit someone once, but he admitted he had it coming and I'm not 
proud of it, or recommending physical violence as a management technique.

wasn't asked to :-). Usually when it gets to that, things have to be 
escalated past the COI and all parties (including the COI) need to sit 
around a table and look at issues.

In general, quitting is an extremely unattractive option and would be a last 
resort, only when everything else has failed.

Pete.

Here's a thought for another discussion... assume a micro-Manager who says 
'do it *this* way, where '*this*' is a fashion which your experience has 
shown will temporarily alleviate the situation... and farther down the 
road make for larger, worse troubles.

Youo say to said Manager 'In accordance with my experience this approach 
will create, in short order, more and expensive problems than it is 
intended to solve.  If your direct order to me is to fix this problem I 
will implement what I have found, after a few decades of experience, what 
will not only fix the problem but not cause future ones.  If your direct 
order to me is do exactly what you say I will write up our interaction 
here and transmit it formally to you and your boss, requesting that we all 
have a nice sit-down to discuss this.  I don't do work that I am not 
willing to put my signature on and I will not sign work which I have good 
grounds to believe will damage your processing.'

The result of that, of course, is said Manager sending Security to watch 
as you clean out your desk.  Is that quitting or being fired?

DD

Yes, indeed-y, and yes I do believer when I have proferred the "let's
kick this upstairs" suggestion I felt like I was well on the road to
"I'll be d*mned if I'm gonna quit, I'm gonna MAKE them fire me!!"

On the other hand, much of what I talk about is That Which Happens in
Due Time, hence my earlier observation that even when I have fired
_my_ manager, my own firing does not happen for another two years, and
then only at contract (non)renewal time, and only with the smokescreen
of one or two other non-renewals along with me.

> if one just wants to get fired one can use a
> wastebasket as a commode.

My Dear Doc, I thought the point of the silly exercise was to get
oneself fired over *principles*, be they monetary or otherwise. So, O
Englightened One, what again is the principle of using a wastebasket
as a commode?

Perhaps it is akin to the proverbial story of the naughty, but ever
exuberant child, who upon arising on Christmas morning to a mountain
of horse dung piled under the tree by his recriminating parents, was
nevertheless excited and joyous, with whoops and yells, as he
excitedly plowed through the excrement with his little hand shovel,
all the while proclaiming, "There can't be this much horsesh*t without
a lil pony being here somewhere!"

Exercise for CLC'ers (and remember to do your own homework) - find the
"pony" in your shop - it's gotta be there somewhere!! :-)

Ken

commodities, becomes more expensive when purchased in smaller increments.  
What you've offered is a good rate for a nine-to-twelve month gig but 
three months means I've got to think about getting my papers out on the 
streets twelve weeks from now... and factor in a couple-four weeks' worth 
of downtime... no, I'm going to need ($x + y)/hr if you're buying only 
three months.'

##### ↳ ↳ Re: OT: Support Documentation

- **From:** Ken <klshafer@att.net>
- **Date:** 2010-09-10T15:00:13-07:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<11e70a89-e6f9-4ac7-9a66-52223dee14f0@j30g2000vbr.googlegroups.com>`
- **References:** `<77572f8d-5ae3-453e-a13b-fdc6c480653d@n3g2000yqb.googlegroups.com> <71644481-473c-47dc-a6cd-85e8bbb62398@n7g2000vbo.googlegroups.com>`

```
On Sep 9, 8:07 am, Alistair <alist...@ld50macca.demon.co.uk> wrote:
> On Sep 1, 12:37 pm, Alistair <alist...@ld50macca.demon.co.uk> wrote:
>
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
> which touches so heavily on IT doesn't attract more attention.

Gosh! I feel so gratified, I'll offer some more!!

>
> It is certainly true that the code represents the single best source
…[5 more quoted lines elided]…
> do.

Not sure what you mean by "in-stream" - comments in code? No matter,
whatever the documentation is (comments in code, external
documentation), it is often wrong, just as you said. Still, if the
flawed documentation is cast at a different level of abstraction, it
can still be useful. See below.

>
> 2.  There is no simple definitive statement as to a program function
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
> avoiding nested IF conditions by the project leader.

At my shop, this problem is fixed pretty simply, and I mean this quite
literally -
1. Locate the offending input record in the input transaction file.
2. Capture a copy of it (maybe :-) ), then delete the record.
3. Re-run the job.

This works because (nearly) all of our jobs don't have DB2 COMMITS,
and the applications are written to force abends when unexpected
events occur, and DB2 is rolled back.

Similar approach on outbound batch - locate the offending key field
(eg. case number), then QMF some status field to suppressing
processing (change OPEN status to CLOSED, and voila! the case is
bypassed).

Then, go to sleep, and work on diagnosing the REAL problem
tomorrow! :-)

>
> 3.  I have more than once come across in-place code which says: Remove
> after (some date in the past). Should it be removed or left in situ?
> Why was the comment and code left in place?

If the code is still active, seems to me to be a matter to point out
to mgmt with a "better research this." If inactive (commented out),
leave the commented out code AND the comments in place. Why? Policies
change, and more than once I have re-activated commented-out code
because the client wanted "things to go back they were."

>
> I have also come across the situation where documentation consisted of
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
> which has been cloned from another program but not updated correctly).

If the docs were written as text or html files, sounds like a job for
grep'ing the entire network drive :-). That may find the original spec
if its file location is far, far away from where the "change request"
documentation is.

>
> Finally, does anyone use UML for batch systems documentation?

Can't answer that directly, but can talk about Use Cases a bit. Though
I have almost NO experience with use cases, after my client started
talking about them, I googled and found a reference from a Rational
guy himself (as opposed to "rational" :-) - of course, he contended
that batch processes COULD be represented in Use Cases, but when you
read this explanation it all a "sleight of hand" whereby the "actor"
was a "batch scheduler" and the "system" was the batch program, and
the "event trigger" was a time/date/clock event that initiated the
"batch scheduler" blah, blah, blah - and he conceded that the
"dialogue" between "actor" and "system" had to be written as a
"separate narrative specification". :-)

Gosh, sounds like traditional or modified Waterfall to me.

On the other hand, what I think are called "state charts" in UML could
be very, VERY useful for batch programs. Elsewhere, in differently
moniker'd methodologies, these might be called Entity Life Cycle
Diagrams (ELCD's), or State Transition Diagrams, a term which fell out
of favor because its acronym could easily be confused with non-
specific urinary tract infections. :-)

Anyway, what a state chart does is describe the events/circumstances
under which the very, very important STATUS variables of a record
change - and hence the record/entity/object attains a new status. Eg.,
an Order can obtain statuses of Entered, Fulfilled (picked), Scheduled-
to-be-shipped, shipped, back-ordered, etc. etc. It is important to
note that the state chart, though drawn like a flow chart, is NOT a
systems flow chart, or a data-flow-diagram (dfd). I tshows the entity
moving through its statuses over time, Sometimes this is called a
"behavioral" model.

Other examples of abstract documentation that provides a good overview
is the "data model" (for all DB's now almost universally represented
as Entity-Relationship-Diagrams (ERD's), and the "process model",
which in the Structured Analysis / Design days were either data-flow-
diagrams (dfd's) or structure charts (Hierarchy-Input-Process-Output,
or HIPO, in IBM-speak). For maintenance purposes, if the HIPO or
structure chart was not created as part of the original documentation,
it is usually not worthwhile to create one, unless the program is
very, very complicated and you just can't get a picture of what is
going on.

For dfd's, the story is different. In my shop, there are hundreds of
batch programs, and they are schedule to run at all different times -
some daily, some weekly (on different days of the week), some monthly
(on different days of the month - 1st Saturday, last Saturday, end-of-
month), and of course the annuals (1st Sat after end-of-year), etc.
etc. Sometimes when I had to track down a problem I had to draw a dfd
just to see how the input/output files were "chained" together,
because the CA7 scheduler Job File, for that particular day, did not
have all the program execution steps that I needed to see - they were
in ANOTHER JCL job to be run on ANOTHER day.

I suppose that the purists would say (and they would be right) that
what I am talking about is a "physical dfd", rather than a "logical
dfd", because it might include sort steps, extract steps
'masquerading' as sorts, backups to GDG's, and the like. But after
all, you're maintaining the CURRENT system, not abstracting out
"essential business logic" to get ready for the replacement system.

So it works OK. And pencil and paper and a photocopier work just fine,
since my shop even limits the number of Visio copies installed (sigh).

Ken
```

#### ↳ Re: Support Documentation

- **From:** "Kerry Liles" <kerry.removethisandoneperiod.liles@gmail.com>
- **Date:** 2010-09-09T14:10:41-04:00

- **Message-ID:** `<i6b7vg$dio$1@news.eternal-september.org>`
- **References:** `<77572f8d-5ae3-453e-a13b-fdc6c480653d@n3g2000yqb.googlegroups.com>`

```
"Alistair" <alistair@ld50macca.demon.co.uk> wrote in message 
news:77572f8d-5ae3-453e-a13b-fdc6c480653d@n3g2000yqb.googlegroups.com...
> Pete (NZ) touched on a subject that may be dear to the hearts of die-
> hard Cobolers and has certainly been a bug-bear for me over the
…[24 more quoted lines elided]…
> documentation for post-implementation support? For BATCH and ONLINE.


An interesting question might be: "Can documentation be harmful?" 
(channelling Dijkstra??)

For example: you encounter a program that you have never seen before and 
there are various written resources available:
- comments in the program
- a writeup about the program in the operations run guide
- a description and flowchart (perhaps) of the program in the department 
documentation pile
etc.
- a document prepared by an outside consultant describing (at great expense 
no doubt) how the 'system' works - including this program.

Is any of this documentation to be trusted? IF (and it may be a big 'if') 
any of it has a reliable timestamp, the recent documentation may be 
considered more reliable than older or un-dated material, but the real 
question is whether or not to trust any of it.

The point of course is that documentation is normally not vetted; there is 
no incentive to keep it correct or even debugged (as there is implied with 
code). Documentation or comments that may have been correct at one time are 
seldom edited to reflect changes or patches or emergency or permanent hacks.

One colleague has opined that the fact that documentation can not be trusted 
is reason enough to not do any!  lol
```

##### ↳ ↳ Re: Support Documentation

- **From:** "Pete Dashwood" <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz>
- **Date:** 2010-09-10T14:14:49+12:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<8etiksFls6U1@mid.individual.net>`
- **References:** `<77572f8d-5ae3-453e-a13b-fdc6c480653d@n3g2000yqb.googlegroups.com> <i6b7vg$dio$1@news.eternal-september.org>`

```
Kerry Liles wrote:
> "Alistair" <alistair@ld50macca.demon.co.uk> wrote in message
> news:77572f8d-5ae3-453e-a13b-fdc6c480653d@n3g2000yqb.googlegroups.com...
…[53 more quoted lines elided]…
> trusted is reason enough to not do any!  lol

This is pretty much the gist of my response to Alistair.

Nevertheless, when delivering tools etc. I spend a great deal of time 
documenting them. I guess "conceptual background" especially when 
graphically illustrated, can be useful to someone who is trying to "get 
their head around" how something is supposed to work.

The point about doing decent diagrams is that a picture can indeed be worth 
a thousand words.

Here's a couple of examples of a diagram which illustrates a concept and 
would take more than a thousand words to explain if written verbally:

http://tinyurl.com/32zo4xr

http://tinyurl.com/35ark85

I have reluctantly come to the conclusion that  (apart from a general 
description at the start of each module or component) comments in code are 
pretty pointless UNLESS you are using a modern facility to generate them, 
manage them, and keep them current.

A MUCH more effective approach is to keep code concise, clear, and succinct. 
("crisp" is a word I have used in presentations on this...)

It is ironic that although COBOL was originally conceived to be 
"self-documenting" (and it has a structure and facilities that lend 
themselves to this), the verbosity of it means it is hard to write crisp 
COBOL code (not impossible, just "hard"...).Style can play a part in this. I 
like to  use whitespace to accentuate and clarify. (And where styles are 
consistently applied, it makes changing code easier too.) For example, I 
split complex performs, nested ifs and complex conditions across lines so 
that each element is clear. (And I always check lengthy complex conditions 
to see if Boolean simplification can be effected, or whether an evaluate 
might be more useful)

Pete.
```

On Sep 14, 5:36 am, marktyers <markty...@gmail.com> wrote:

It has nothing to do with the _language_ (COBOL). There are several
implementations, commercial ones, where the setup is quite easy. But
then you pay for those so they pay people to build the installers.

OpenCOBOL is a voluntary group that does this for free and provides
the compiler to anyone who wants it. Binaries are available, from the
FAQ:

----------------------------------
1.5   Are there pre-built OpenCOBOL packages

Yes. Debian APT, and RPM packages exist. Packages for NetBSD. Many.
Google opencobol packages for any late breaking news.

A Debian Advanced Package Tool binary package exists for OpenCOBOL 1.0
as open-cobol and lists dependencies of

    * libc6 (>= 2.7-1),
    * libcob1,
    * libcob1-dev (= 1.0-1),
    * libdb4.5 (>= 4.5.20-3),
    * libdb4.5-dev,
    * libgmp3-dev,
    * libgmp3c2,
    * libltdl3-dev,
    * libncurses5 (>= 5.6+20071006-3)

Thanks to the gracious efforts of Bart Martens, bartm on Debian's .org
domain.
1.5.1   kiska.net repository

Also check out kiska.net for binary builds on various platforms.
Thanks to Sergey Kashyrin.
-----------------------------------

It just happens that none of these are "simple installer for [MacOS]
platform!"


> I would be happy to work with anyone who is prepared to support the
> preparation of an OS X package, maybe using MacPorts. Are there any
> takers?

If you post this on the OpenCOBOL forum, or communicate with those who
have posted details of how they installed on Mac then I am sure they
will welcome your help.
```

- **References:** `<e8a1576c-20c3-4289-b78e-6068ccb5d875@j8g2000yqd.googlegroups.com> <1jnutsp.np0gee1hifo1nN%davide.grandi@mclink.it> <4076f432-ea44-4ed2-813f-19609383fb6f@x42g2000yqx.googlegroups.com> <1282931884@f1.n250.z2.fidonet.ftn> <7e07e615-2c94-45a5-96f7-0ca796cdcf8d@h19g2000yqb.googlegroups.com> <29f000fe-2f1e-4386-abcc-272bb56cafb2@x42g2000yqx.googlegroups.com> <a993ad5a-428a-4ebd-925c-dbb6939f937c@s19g2000vbr.googlegroups.com> <df4dc2ee-7198-4094-8f72-75ad82070e79@s17g2000prh.googlegroups.com>`

```
<all snipped>

Just to follow on to this thread.

The OpenCOBOL "development" effort seems (to me) to assume and work best 
in/with a UNIX/Linux environment. Installing there (and adding the tools 
related to it and samples for it) is a "piece of cake".

Because of the popularity (for many, not all) of Windows. There are also 
relatively easy instructions for Cygwin and Mingw versions of OpenCOBOL 
(including, among other things, "binaries" for Mingw).

Yes, there are other operating systems and YES, people have tried (and 
succeeded) in doing installs on them. Linux under z/VM on IBM mainframes for 
example *and* OSX for Mac and Warp (or whatever the OS/2 thingy is called 
today).

The fact that the OS's in that latter category haven't (so far) had 
sufficient "demand" from those who can to (and know how to) build "simple 
installers" simply means that those are NOT the primary operating systems 
either for those involved in the OpenCOBOL development or the OC usage.

As others have pointed out, the best place to "get in touch" with those who 
might be interested in or helpful with such developments is in the OpenCOBOL 
forum and not this newsgroup. Yes, this there are people in this newsgroup 
who use and/or know OC, but it is NOT the primary place to solicit input or 
discuss OC issues. OBVIOUSLY, you are welcome to post whatever you want 
here, but if your goal is to get the (on average) quickest and most accurate 
responses for OC-only topics, I really do recommend that OC forum.
```

…[26 more quoted lines elided]…
> responses for OC-only topics, I really do recommend that OC forum.

I was hopeful with OS X as it is a 'UNIX environment'. And I can
confirm it is as far from being a piece of cake as it is possible to
get. I have spent many 'happy' hours discussing the issue on the
openCOBOL forums, getting obtuse answers that assume you have a degree
in computer science. I can only conclude that COBOL users have no
interest in getting new users to learn the language. This is in direct
contrast to every other language I have installed and played with,
where installation has been a breeze, allowing me to focus on the
programming language.

I am sure my frustrations and countless wasted hours are due to my
inexperience with UNIX but I was hopeful that someone would understand
that not everyone who wants to program is a computer scientist with a
vast understanding of UNIX.

I have now removed COBOL from the teaching curriculum and will be
teaching my students other languages.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Simple Installation on OS X

_(thread depth: 10)_

- **From:** "Pete Dashwood" <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz>
- **Date:** 2010-09-15T14:09:31+12:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<8fao6tFd3oU1@mid.individual.net>`
- **References:** `<e8a1576c-20c3-4289-b78e-6068ccb5d875@j8g2000yqd.googlegroups.com> <1jnutsp.np0gee1hifo1nN%davide.grandi@mclink.it> <4076f432-ea44-4ed2-813f-19609383fb6f@x42g2000yqx.googlegroups.com> <1282931884@f1.n250.z2.fidonet.ftn> <7e07e615-2c94-45a5-96f7-0ca796cdcf8d@h19g2000yqb.googlegroups.com> <29f000fe-2f1e-4386-abcc-272bb56cafb2@x42g2000yqx.googlegroups.com> <a993ad5a-428a-4ebd-925c-dbb6939f937c@s19g2000vbr.googlegroups.com> <df4dc2ee-7198-4094-8f72-75ad82070e79@s17g2000prh.googlegroups.com> <R%ujo.125206$mN7.124253@en-nntp-08.dc1.easynews.com> <26c32413-4cf2-4b02-8f75-d515a1851eea@v23g2000vbi.googlegroups.com>`

```
marktyers wrote:
> On Sep 13, 8:33 pm, "Bill Klein" <wmkl...@noreply.ix.netcom.com>
> wrote:
…[48 more quoted lines elided]…
> teaching my students other languages.

That may be no bad thing.

Sorry to see your experience Mark, but at least you have documented it 
honestly.

Sometimes things work out for the best. I don't think the failure to have 
COBOL knowledge will impair the future careers of any of your students, and 
it is one thing less for them to disperse their energy and attention over.

Nevertheless, There are a couple of observations that occurred to me as I 
read your post, so I'll mention them.

QUOTE:

I have spent many 'happy' hours discussing the issue on the
openCOBOL forums, getting obtuse answers that assume you have a degree
in computer science.

UNQUOTE:

Whether or not one has an actual degree in computer science, it is fair and 
reasonable to expect that someone teaching programming is capable of doing 
so, and will have a large enough experience that they can deal with computer 
related problems. Many people find that there is a large gap between the 
acquisition of skill in a language and the actual implementation of 
applications in a live environment. This is because the learning of a 
programming language is pretty much an academic classroom exercise, but 
actually developing applications means coming to grips with a working 
environment and sometimes that environment is much more complex than the 
language.

If your lack of Unix experience meant you couldn't understand what people in 
the forum were telling you, I see two options (not mutually exclusive):

1. Ask for clarification in simple terms or at a level you can deal with.
2. Do some homework on Unix so you can get more understanding.

Obviously, Unix is very large subject, but you could limit your interest to 
the deployment of packages, and specifically, the Open COBOL package. Would 
it be fair to say that you went to the forum looking for a solution you 
could simply run?

That isn't necessarily how it works. This is not commercial software where 
you pay money to make the problems go away. This is intended for you to do 
the work yourself so that you can learn from it and so it doesn't involve 
cost. Yes, there are people who have gone before and are happy to leave 
their experience for the benefit of others, but it appears this hasn't 
happened yet with Mac OSX.

Another alternative to these two options is to simply scrub round it and 
forget Open COBOL. If you were determined to include COBOL in your course, 
there are student versions available but they may not be designed for Mac 
OSX. So, you either emulate Windows or you run a Virtual machine. 
(Ironically, the Virtual Machines I run are also Open Source (Sun 
originally, now Oracle) but it runs on a Windows platform with no trouble 
whatsoever.)

While the Mac is an excellent computing platform, perhaps it is not the 
wisest choice if you are going to teach programming to people who will use 
this knowledge in a world where less than 6% of the computers in use are 
Macs. 
(http://arstechnica.com/microsoft/news/2010/01/windows-7-growing-faster-than-vista-overtakes-mac-os.ars) 
It is hardly surprising there can be problems in getting software.

Bill outlined this gently and fairly in his post which you quoted above.

Ask yourself this:  If I hadn't chosen Mac as the platform for my students, 
would I be having the problems I am?

Answer:  Probably not with regard to COBOL, but most other things are not 
problematic.

Why is COBOL different? Because it is an expensive, archaic commercial 
language, that was not intended for free distribution. (c.f.  Java, C#, 
etc.). A (small) team of enthusiasts devoted thousands of hours to providing 
a free Open Source version of a language they loved and are committed to. 
With the tiny resources available (compared to other languages and 
platforms) they simply can't provide everything for everybody. The hope is 
that others will seize the torch and add value to their efforts. 
Unfortunately, this doesn't seem to be happening, but that is nobody's 
fault. Not yours, not theirs, not the rest of the COBOL community's.

It is a simple matter of commercial reality. And the reality is that nobody 
has a pressing need for a free version of COBOL, apart from hobbyists, and 
nostalgic Old Timers.

The people who are using COBOL commercially have been spending money on it 
for so long that they are used to doing that and have no problem with 
coughing up for the latest version of the compiler (whether they actually 
need it or not - I am staggered by the mails I get from people who are 
unhappy about paying for a .NET COBOL compiler, but buy it anyway (because 
they are told they should). Invariably, they don't need it, because they are 
moving off COBOL and they can run their legacy code perfectly well on .NET 
by adding a dozen or so lines of code to it...Surprisingly (or maybe 
not...), there are no refunds or even credit against other products/tools; 
you buy it, you got it.)

Small wonder that the COBOL market is shrinking. (Apart from the fact that 
it implements an outmoded paradigm based on centralized processing, it is 
probably the most expensive language to develop and maintain computer 
systems in, so more and more people are looking for alternatives. At least 
there ARE alternatives now, and some of them are far better than COBOL...)

OC is an attempt to make a boutique version of what was once the world's 
most popular programming language. It is a noble effort, but for people like 
yourself who have chosen a "minority" platform, it simply can't accommodate 
you unless you make a lot more (directed) effort than you have shown to 
date.

I can understand your decision to drop it.

QUOTE:

"I can only conclude that COBOL users have no interest in getting new users 
to learn the language."

UNQUOTE

Why would it be the responsibility of COBOL users to encourage you or anyone 
else to use the language? They are not on commission. People use a computer 
programming language because it meets their needs, not because everyone else 
is using it or it has a high "friends" count on Facebook. Sure, people who 
share an interest often like to discuss things (this forum is a case in 
point) but if you were expecting to be wooed and courted into using Open 
COBOL that seems a bit unrealistic. Most of the sites still using COBOL are 
doing so because they need to. Nobody likes being in a position where they 
need to do something (we prefer to have options...) So the people working on 
these sites have employment guaranteed for the immediate future, but there 
is a shadow over it and, in the long term these jobs will disappear. Would 
you encourage a young person looking for a career to get into COBOL? No? 
Neither would many of us.

BOTTOM LINE:

1. You were looking for a free version of COBOL that would run on a platform 
which has less than 6% of the market.
2.  While this was not impossible, there was a degree of effort required to 
make it happen.
3.  You decided the effort involved was more that you were prepared to make. 
Fair enough. Your call. But don't blame the COBOL people for not persuading 
you or specifically trying to meet your needs.
4. In the broad scheme of things (and this is the most important thing of 
all, more important than choice of platform or difficulties with Unix, or 
any technical problems...) your students will NOT suffer if you don't cover 
COBOL in your course.

Pete.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Simple Installation on OS X

_(thread depth: 11)_

- **From:** Alistair <alistair@ld50macca.demon.co.uk>
- **Date:** 2010-09-15T08:04:49-07:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<f44ed440-fa75-49f0-b730-85e90a9d4004@f26g2000vbm.googlegroups.com>`

I think it MAY be worth noting a "passing remark" that the OP made close to 
the end of this thread.  I believe that he said that he would be teaching 16 
year olds.  Now this may or may not mean the same thing to those outside of 
the US, but in the US, this means that (almost certainly) that he is 
teaching in a High School.

To me, there is a world of difference between what I would expect from a 
college/university (even junior college) instructor and what I would expect 
from a high school teacher. Without knowing in detail the "worlk load" of 
this teacher, the chances are fairly slim that he would be teaching a full 
load of "computer related" courses - unless some of them are "computer 
literacy" courses.  It is also unlikely that this course is an introductory 
course on the way to a track that would end up with a "computer 
professional".

The desire for an "already built" system that could easily be distributed to 
students - and the lack of ability to follow OpenCOBOL forum information - 
seems more uinderstandable in a High School teaching environment.  I also 
tend to agree with Pete that this is probably NOT a place where COBOL is 
necesarily a "good fit" for introducign students to procedural coding.  (I 
do think that an introduction to at lest one procedural rather than OO 
language seems like a "good idea" - but I would think another choice, 
possibly "C" or even (traditional) Basic would be better)

Depening upon the background of the students and the nature of the High 
School curriculum, where a "programming language" course would fit in, may 
vary tremendously (as would the knowledge and background of an instructor of 
such courses).

Others may feel that all of this is irrelevant, but I will certainly admit 
that some of my responses were made under the (misunderstanding that this 
would be for a college-level course.  Today's hish school students 
(especially those who would want to take such a coures) is very different 
than thoe "of my day" - but adding COBOL to heir world view does seem a 
relatively low-priority to me.

My point was that if you are training people for the world of commerce it is 
probably a good thing to train them on the platform they are likely to use.

A platform with less than 6% of the market hardly qualifies.

a good case for letting them use whatever platform they have.

Virtual Machines make this perfectly viable.

on most platforms. COBOL is commercial and designed for the platforms that 
are likely to need it (not including Mac). It is also fair to say that COBOL 
(at least Open COBOL) COULD be installed on the Mac, it just requires more 
effort than the poster was prepared to make.

- **From:** Derek Schrock <derekschrock@gmail.com>

- **Message-ID:** `<0848dc74-354f-431c-b4c6-f0b97daf8e07@d8g2000yqf.googlegroups.com>`
- **References:** `<098a698a-a3da-4e55-b6b5-12a58ee646f9@d8g2000yqf.googlegroups.com> <f676deda-3d7e-4157-95ca-d74902256a2c@5g2000yqz.googlegroups.com> <8cv9ahFa0uU1@mid.individual.net>`

```
On Aug 17, 7:15 am, "Pete Dashwood"
<dashw...@removethis.enternet.co.nz> wrote:
> Derek Schrock wrote:
> > On Aug 12, 9:13 pm, Ken <klsha...@att.net> wrote:
…[97 more quoted lines elided]…
> "I used to write COBOL...now I can do anything."


So the idea here is to find all instances of "string" plus the
context?

If so you could use grep's -n (assuming you have that option).  This
will print the line number along with the matched line.  Using this
information you could write a complementary awk program to pull the
context.

For instance:
[dereks@ircbsd ~]$ grep -n FILE foo.*
foo.c:19:    FILE* f = fopen( filename, "rb" );
...

We know that foo.c has reference to "FILE" on line 19.
With the awk script you would store old lines before and after line
19.
```
