```
Bill Klein wrote:
> To see the videao, try this link
> 
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
> information, but that is the page to get to the video (I think)

Yes, that worked fine, thanks.

I viewed the video on full screen and it was blurry with poor sound, but I 
could see what was going on.

There was nothing here that made me go "Wow!" and all of the facilities 
(including the development tools) I already have, or can get with a simple 
download and plug in to VS 2008. (For free...)

It made me smile to hear the presenter getting excited about stuff that most 
.NET programmers take for granted and have been taking for granted for many 
years now. I described these facilites here years ago but I don't think 
anyone actually believed me... :-) I was also amused that the first sample 
app. was a post to Twitter (which didn't actually work properly, but was 
intended to show that COBOL is now "cool" (it isn't :-))

Nevertheless, it is a good integration, as we would expect, given it was 
done by MF and given that VS is designed to have such integrations plugged 
into it.

It is kind of nice to see COBOL getting a facelift but, in my opinion it is 
way too late. If this had been done 15 years ago we might have a different 
IT world.

I see two major problems with it:

1. It doesn't look like COBOL any more and programmers who have resisted OO 
will find it very strange indeed. Despite the "spin" that MF have "updated 
and trimmed down the language to support .Net" (in other words, tried to 
make it less verbose), what we have is something that will spook the 
majority of COBOL people who are deeply embedded in COBOL. (Shorthand forms 
of OO notation are STILL more verbose than the free languages, and although 
the presenter says it will be easy for C# and VB programmers to move to 
COBOL (true), I wouldn't hold my breath. It is MUCH more likely that the 
converse will occur...

Like so many of the COBOL community, this presenter has failed to realise 
that the underlying paradigm is flawed and suffers by comparison with other 
languages in a distributed environment. (But that is too subtle an argument 
for anyone who makes their living out of COBOL so I don't blame him for 
that.) Once people master OO COBOL they will soon find it is more tedious to 
write than the free languages. (This was very much my own experience, but 
I'd be surprised if others don't find the same.)

The fact is that with a highly advanced IDE, the choice of language really 
isn't that important (provided it can handle Objects). Nevertheless, 
languages designed around OO are always going to be less verbose and more 
facile at handling Classes and Objects, than a language that has had these 
facilities "bolted on".

2. It is competing with products that have all the same facilities (plus 
more) and are FREE.

I can't see a traditional COBOL guy shelling out thousands of dollars to get 
.Net COBOL (from MF or Alchemy) then finding that it doesn't look like 
COBOL, he has to master concepts and facilities which he already fiercely 
resisted when OO COBOL was first released, and once he has mastered it 
(given that he manages to...), realising he could have got the same 
facilities for free.

However, as I mentioned elsewhere, I get mail from people who want out of 
COBOL and most of them are companies (not individuals) who have bought a 
.NET COBOL compiler because they were told to and because they have always 
used COBOL. (Some are not; they are non-COBOL people who are interested in 
moving a COBOL site to .NET)

So much for an objective look, now let's view it as an existing COBOL shop 
might:

1. It provides a superb IDE which makes COBOL much easier and quicker to 
develop.

2. There is some learning required, but this can be easily absorbed into the 
cost of implementing the IDE.

3. COBOL guys now are prepared to take another look at OO (even if they 
wouldn't have a bar of it 15 years ago) because they realise it is a very 
successful paradigm. (What they usually don't realise is WHY it is, but that 
comes after a few years of using it...)

4. Existing COBOL shops are used to spending money for software and accept 
that a major upgrade will incur a cost, so the money probably isn't much of 
an objection. (It IS to small businesses and individual developers, but 
these are NOT who it is targeted at.) From what I gather, the average cost 
of implementing a .NET COBOL compiler, with support, maintenance, and 
licensing is around $US11,000 (This is REAL cost, not the quoted price.It is 
based on information from 3 sites, in different parts of the world).

To a small business, this is cause for pause, to a larger one it is petty 
cash in the IT budget. To be fair, If you decided to drop COBOL and go to 
the free .NET languages, there is still a learning cost involved. I assess 
it at as around $US2000. In my own case, I never paid a cent. I used free 
videos and taught myself, but many people won't want to do that. And today I 
employ people who are being taught C# at University or Polytech. (And they 
are easier to get and cost much less than COBOL guys;that is why I do most 
of the COBOL stuff myself.)

5. There is a tempting vision that mainframe (or other procedural COBOL) 
code can be simply compiled for .NET and all your worries are over. Anyone 
who buys that, really deserves whatever they get.

BOTTOM LINE (as always, one man's opinion):

1. MF have done a good job of integrating COBOL with VS.
2. It is really too late to change the perception of COBOL as a modern OO 
language, ideal for distributed and networked solutions, but it will give 
some encouragement to people starting to look at where to go next with 
COBOL.
3. It remains to be seen whether the COBOL community will now embrace OO 
COBOL, given there is a decent IDE for it. (They didn't when it was 
available for Fujitsu, but MF have a larger COBOL market share.)
4.I believe this will encourage some programmers and sites away from COBOL 
rather than persuade C#, Java, and VB.NET programmers to move to COBOL.

Pete.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Not (intended to be) a "Commercial"

_(thread depth: 7)_

- **From:** "Pete Dashwood" <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz>
- **Date:** 2010-09-19T12:46:24+12:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<8fl4r3Fkp6U1@mid.individual.net>`
- **References:** `<GbBko.157427$Is6.93397@en-nntp-13.dc1.easynews.com> <8fhil7FohoU1@mid.individual.net> <kHRko.162710$mN7.155152@en-nntp-08.dc1.easynews.com> <8fiv2tF8abU1@mid.individual.net> <BuYko.164603$mN7.5178@en-nntp-08.dc1.easynews.com> <8fjl7bF2e6U1@mid.individual.net> <28376961-da7b-49ec-aa83-0d67b5bdd45e@a19g2000vbi.googlegroups.com>`

```
Alistair wrote:
> On Sep 18, 12:13 pm, "Pete Dashwood"
> <dashw...@removethis.enternet.co.nz> wrote:
…[9 more quoted lines elided]…
>   simplified OO code

The "shorthand forms" of OO notation for COBOL are NOT MF proprietary. The 
double colon notation has been part of OO COBOL since the mid-90s.

>   intellisense

Nothing to do with COBOL or MF. Has been part of Visual Studio since it was 
released.

>   ability to hide/collapse sections to make it easier to see relevant
> chunks of code

Nothing to do with COBOL or MF. Has been part of Visual Studio since it was 
released.


>   VS inclusion of copy files improved to cater for Cobol's
> COPY...REPLACING capability

No, the include facility has always been part of VS. The COPY ...REPLACING 
is part of the tailoring that VS is designed to accept for plugins.

>   addition of Cobol snippets of code

Nothing to do with COBOL or MF. Has been part of Visual Studio since it was 
released. Snippets are availabe for all .NET languages , and you can add 
your own easily to the library.

>   templates (the FLV didn't show what these were)

Nothing to do with COBOL or MF. Has been part of Visual Studio since it was 
released.

Templates allow you to set up a project with a few mouse clicks. There are 
extensive templates for all of the .NET languages. For example, there are 
web site templates, desktop templates, command line templates (that allow 
you to develop programs without a GUI), Windows service templates, .DLL 
templates, and so on. You simply select the template for what you want to do 
and VS sets up a project for that with code stubs and initialization code 
all written for you. Now there are COBOL templates if you buy the MF 
product.


>   mention of Analyser Express (which might be an add-in product but
> I'm not sure).

No, I'm not sure about this either.

The point is that all of these fantastic features have been available to 
.NET programmers for over 10 years now. (I think Richard pointed out in a 
previous post that many of the facilities in VS were available in other 
software before VS, too.)

It's only COBOL that has been the "poor relation". Fujitsu attempted to 
address it with their NetCOBOL for .NET product, and now MF have done so. It 
is good to see.

>
> Other points:
>   CICS, IMS, DB2 not supported yet but promised for the future

DB2 can be used with VS. I have done it using ODBC.

>   NET Express upgrades available
>   NET Express will continue to be supported but there are no plans to
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
>   wrappers available to facilitate refactoring

It is VERY difficult to automate COBOL code refactoring, although PRIMA 
(http://primacomputing.co.nz) are getting very close. We have FULLY 
AUTOMATED data and program code conversion from indexed files to Relational 
database. You can have a completely normalized RDB generated and loaded with 
your existing data, a data access layer of objects that manage the new RDB 
and run anywhere (desktop, web site, or called from existing applications - 
whether these applications are OO or not), and your existing applications 
changed from indexed access (WITHOUT changing the existing processing 
logic - in effect the programs function as they always have, but now they 
"see" the RDB instead of the indexed data sets), to invoke the new objects 
in the Data Access Layer, all without any manual coding being required.

We are getting a base of sites that have done it and each one adds to our 
knowledge and improves the toolset.

Obviously, accomplishing full automation of this means it can be done very 
quickly and without errors. (It took 4 years for me to design and write this 
software.)

The next step is to extract and reuse COBOL code. (Refactoring). Visual 
Studio does have facilities that support this, but it is still a complex 
process. So far, it needs manual intervention and an experienced COBOL 
programmer to do it successfully, but we are trialing some tools that 
certainly help the process.

I'm not sure how far I want to go with this. It is very difficult (but so 
was the stuff outlined above, and people in this forum told me it couldn't 
be done due to the huge variation in COBOL coding styles and standards. They 
were almost right...)  I don't want to spend a large amount of time and 
money developing something that nobody wants :-)  Howard has pointed out 
that many sites will prefer to re-develop from scratch or use a package or 
outsourced approach, rather than refactor their existing systems into a more 
modern environment. I think he is right, but it would be nice for people to 
have the option.

Converting COBOL to Java is not what I would call refactoring, although some 
people use the term loosely.

I'd like to see something that can take existing  COBOL modules 
(sub-programs) and turn them into Classes (that is actually not too hard), 
and also have something that can analyze COBOL source and pull out PERFORMed 
routines to make them Object Methods of a Class (this is MUCH more 
problematic...)

Being a small company with limited resources, PRIMA has to focus very 
carefully on what is worth developing. Currently, improvements and 
enhancements to the existing toolset (usually based on user feedback) would 
take priority over refactoring, but it is an important "back burner" 
project.



>   MF are working on solutions for RM Cobol and Acucobol users
>   DIALOG is not part of Visual Cobol. MF are considering options.

This is a bit like the PowerCOBOL dilemma for Fujitsu customers.

PowerCOBOL is an excellent "QuickBuild" GUI tool for COBOL and many people 
adopted it. (I get mail asking for help with it about once a fortnight/3 
weeks and most of this mail is from South America where it seems to enjoy 
considerable popularity.)

The problem is that it is going nowhere and there is a question mark over 
its future and support. Alchemy are silent on it.

I considered writing a toolset to convert PowerCOBOL to .NET but there 
simply wasn't any interest and it is a lot of work.  The processes described 
above for migrating to RDB are supported by the PRIMA toolset for PowerCOBOL 
and we currently have a client migrating a moderate size codebase of 
PowerCOBOL away from indexed files.

It wouldn't be too hard to automate the conversion of a PowerCOBOL project 
into .NET, but again, there is a lot of time involved.

DIALOG has the same problem. You can easily convert the screens to WPF but 
the event code needs to be translated... (In PowerCOBOL the event code is in 
COBOL; in DIALOG it is in a MF scripting language.)

FINALLY:  If anyone reading this has a PowerCOBOL codebase and would be 
interested in working with us to get it into .NET, PLEASE contact me via the 
website noted above.

As per the topic of this thread, this is not intended to be a commercial, 
but I would be interested in talking about possibilities with anyone 
seriously interested.

Pete
```

On Sat, 18 Sep 2010 23:13:44 +1200, "Pete Dashwood"
<dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz> wrote:

>It is kind of nice to see COBOL getting a facelift but, in my opinion it is 
>way too late. If this had been done 15 years ago we might have a different 
>IT world.

I sincerely doubt it.

>I see two major problems with it:
>
>1. It doesn't look like COBOL any more and programmers who have resisted OO 
>will find it very strange indeed.

The population of people who really "got" OO by upgrading their CoBOL
environment is really small.   Most of us needed to switch
environments completely.   Part of this is because the jobs we were
used to really didn't benefit from OO - without being integrated into
an OO environment.   So we learned with a different language in an
environment that was better suited for OO.

Now that we have selected a good tool for this new environment, and
have learned how to use it - we really don't need to go back to CoBOL
in this new environment.   We *still* don't need OO CoBOL in that 30
year old mainframe report.

Agreed. That was my point. If "everybody" had embraced OO COBOL the way they 
embraced "structured programming" for example, then it is possible to 
postulate that there may not have been the environment switches which you

…[3 more quoted lines elided]…

Some did. Most didn't. Instead they simply adopted other solutions 
(packaging, outsourcing, etc.). This was never possible before and people 
really HAD to run their own IT shops, for most of the latter half of the 
last century. With the advent of standard software like spreadsheets and 
databases at an individual level, with the free transmission of data around 
the Intranet, centralized IT became more about tech support and keeping 
things going, than it was about application development. A new generation of 
more computer literate people arrived and used personal computers as a 
matter of course to assist them in their jobs.

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Not (intended to be) a "Commercial"

_(thread depth: 8)_

- **From:** Howard Brazee <howard@brazee.net>
- **Date:** 2010-09-21T09:37:05-06:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<d2jh9611nditcij3vh0keu3jd22tq3vkqj@4ax.com>`
- **References:** `<GbBko.157427$Is6.93397@en-nntp-13.dc1.easynews.com> <8fhil7FohoU1@mid.individual.net> <kHRko.162710$mN7.155152@en-nntp-08.dc1.easynews.com> <8fiv2tF8abU1@mid.individual.net> <BuYko.164603$mN7.5178@en-nntp-08.dc1.easynews.com> <8fjl7bF2e6U1@mid.individual.net> <7kse96p4kccr6f0jg1t3c1bgfj0vccp9r3@4ax.com> <8frlvoFc8oU1@mid.individual.net>`

```
On Wed, 22 Sep 2010 00:15:49 +1200, "Pete Dashwood"
<dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz> wrote:

>> The population of people who really "got" OO by upgrading their CoBOL
>> environment is really small.
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
>mention. Can't tell for sure, of course.

In a system with spaghetti code CoBOL programs, replacing them with
structured code CoBOL programs doesn't change the system.    In many
cases, we have both being written side-by-side.   Structured makes the
program, easier to maintain, but it doesn't really do anything about
the system.    

There is a downside in touching the system - what we don't touch, we
don't break.   But OO programs really aren't worth much if we don't go
whole hog with an OO environment.   At least, they aren't worth
enough.

So CoBOL isn't dying out by being replaced with OO CoBOL or any other
language in existing environments.   Instead it is dying out (or being
pushed into the background) by replacing systems.    We don't have
horses adapting to freeways here.

In my shop, we switched from mainframe CoBOL & IDMS into a web &
database Oracle/PeopleSoft system.    The whole old paradigm is being
left behind.

The new system does have some CoBOL that is now OO, but it isn't our
CoBOL.    In fact, one of the big selling points is that we won't need
hardly any application programmers (Isn't that what they said when
CoBOL was invented?).   And what they will be mostly responsible for
is installing package upgrades.   Instead, they will have lots of
database people, security people, infrastructure people, and users
writing their own data warehouse reports.   Users are supposed to
adapt to the system instead of the other way around.

>Yes, I agree that your reasons for OO COBOL missing the boat may be just as 
>valid as mine, but we will never know.

Ditto.   But we can analyze both theories and see how what we learn
can fit into changes that come in the future.    

I've had enough experience to not believe it when someone claims "this
time we have it right".    OO won't be the end-all that some people
claim - at least not as we see it today.   Experience we have acquired
in our current change should not be wasted.

I love the title of this book _This Time Is Different: Eight Centuries
of Financial Folly_ by Carmen M. Reinhart.   I saw it in the book
store and mentioned it to my banker son-in-law.   He read it and said
it might be too dry for my light reading, full of charts and stuff -
he appreciated it though.    Still, it is a lovely title.
```

(This was told by the same instructor who asked 'What is the definition of 
a computer-user?  A computer-user is someone who, when prompted for a 'y 
or n' response, keys in the numeral '8'; write *all* of your user 
applications with this in mind and your users will love you.')

DD
```

Rene_Surop wrote:
>>COBOL would need pic X(nnnn), I would think. Check if MF COBOL handles
>>BLOBs; that would be another option.
…[16 more quoted lines elided]…
> I will search for more. Thanks again.

First time I came across BLOB was over ten years ago when Thane Hubbell
quizzed me about BLOBs with reference to the intended COBOL Standards
Collection classes - which as you know never happened.

Then your using 'Object Reference' above piqued my interest. Get
yourself into the new M/F KnowledgeBase and query with the word BLOB.
Several entries but I picked up on BLOB of a jpg. The project (N/E 4.0
written in 2006) is BlobDemo.zip. Looks fairly straight forward - a
procedural program. However there may be features in N/E 4.0 that you
and I are not aware of, but for sure you can't use the N/E 4.0
application file with N/E 3 - they aren't backwards compatible

Assuming there is nothing weird and wonderful in 4.0 ignore the App file
and create a new App file for N/E 3. Here's a brief extract from the
Readme file :-

----------------------------------------------
INTRODUCTION

This Demo reads in the binary data of a .jpg, (*** da Vinci's 'Mona
Lisa' ***), and then writes it to a database 8KB at a time.  The .jpg is
stored in the database as an Image field. It then reads this Image field
8KB at a time into a memory buffer and then writes it to a file on disk
and then opens the file.

SOURCE FILES:

BlobDemo.cbl
SQLCA.CPY (*** M/F copyfile)
WINDOWS.CPY (*** M/F copyfile)

REQUIREMENTS:
This demo works with SQL Server.  Create an ODBC data source with the
name BlobDB. Add the stored procedures to the database.
	
OPERATION:
After you have set up the ODBC DSN and added the stored procedures to
the database, run the program.  It will create a table in the database
and then write the binary data from the .jpg to the table.  It will then
read this binary data from the table into a memory buffer and write it
to disk as a new .jpg file. It will then open this new .jpg file.

---------------------------------------------------------------------------

If writing the 'chunks' of 8K presents any problems, (it is using
CBL_WRITE routines), I'll try and dig out a copy of Alan Wheeler's
original WRITE-CHUNKS.cbl, (or whatever it was called). There might be a
copy of that program on the old forum if you cross-reference
correspondence between Alan and myself about 'chunk--writing'.

If you get stuck OO-wise regarding the WSDL - sing out and I'll help if
I can. If you can't get into that KnowledgeBase - write me and I'll tell
you how to get it.

Jimmy, Calgary AB
```

#### ↳ Re: Sending JPG file using Cobol WebService

- **From:** Emerson <emersonlopes@gmail.com>
- **Date:** 2010-09-21T08:59:41-07:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<3dfe9c1d-90b3-471e-905a-1e2e6879b033@q18g2000vbm.googlegroups.com>`
- **References:** `<35e9465e-a18b-49e1-9cdb-7df0c060d2b1@l32g2000prn.googlegroups.com>`

```
Hello,

You cannot transmit raw binary data as part of SOAP XML message. You
need to use one of the following methods:

1. transform JPEG into S/MIME type and then to transmit it as a
regular string (doubles image size)
2. use base64 encode to transform jpeg binary into something suitable
to be transmitted along the SOAP xml (50% more data)
3. send images as an attachment appended to your SOAP packet (this
would be easy with Cobol.net)

In either case the other end needs to know how to handle the data that
you are sending in order to recreate the jpeg. You also need a very
large PIC X field in this case, long enough to hold JPEG encoded
content.

Since you are using Netexpress you can use MSXML to base64 encode the
jpeg image, but again, it depends on web service provider chosen
method to deal with jpeg (I'm assume you are just consuming the web
service).


Here is a VB example that could be adapted to use with Netexpress $OLE
bridge.

http://www.nonhostile.com/howto-encode-decode-base64-vb6.asp

Regards, Emerson


On 17 set, 00:46, Rene_Surop <infodynamics...@yahoo.com> wrote:
> I know that Cobol programmers are sending data into the Webservice.
> This is done by referencing Cobol data types. Lately I have been
…[9 more quoted lines elided]…
> Any ideas?
```

#### ↳ Re: Lets stop MF and MS bashing and start bashing Intel....

- **From:** "Pete Dashwood" <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz>
- **Date:** 2010-09-22T21:30:26+12:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<8fu0llFpa0U1@mid.individual.net>`
- **References:** `<aeb46457-7819-4711-99a8-a59ddf74b46e@u13g2000vbo.googlegroups.com>`

```
Alistair wrote:
> The following appeared on the BBC News web site:
>
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
> the 90s.

re the topic of this thread, personally, I'm not into bashing anybody.

As for this report, it just makes me laugh that people are outraged and 
shocked at something which has been an industry practice since the late 
1960s.

I remember being quite shocked  (in the late 1960s) when we upgraded our 
mainframe to find that the upgrade actually took 5 minutes (removing the 
brick that was slowing down the processor) and then 9 hours to run 
diagnostics and check everything was OK. It was done on a Saturday morning 
and I happened to be at work. I was always interested in what the engineers 
had to do and the engineer who performed the upgrade was a friend. He 
explained what the process actually entailed. When I expressed my 
disapproval at what seemed like a shoddy practice he explained it like this:

" Let's say you've spent large sums of money inventing a processor chip that 
has amazing capabilities. It is expensive to produce but if you do large 
scale production the price falls significantly. There is only a limited 
market for the top end capabilities of your chip but a considerable market 
for less powerful versions of it. You can't sell the full capability for the 
same price as the limited one, because people are not stupid and they will 
simply buy the cheap version and use it for top end applications You COULD 
have a number of different versions of your chip so that there is a 
marketing range, but that means you need separate production runs and the 
costs for everybody across the range would be higher. Instead, we mass 
produce a single chip. Then we slow it down to provide a marketing range. 
Say models 10, 20, 30, 40 etc. The chip in the 10 is exactly the same chip 
as is in the 40 but it has a circuit board ('brick' in the trade) which 
limits its capability. You want an upgrade? we replace the brick with a 
different one that gives you the new model. We haven't charged you for the 
full capability of the chip so it isn't really unfair, and by doing this, we 
can keep the cost of production down so that everybody who buys our 
equipment (from top to bottom of the range) gets a benefit."

Whether you are persuaded by his argument or not, the fact is that every 
major manufacturer of computer equipment has implemented this approach in 
some form or another.

Before you decide to take a pair of pliers and upgrade your mainfame to the 
maximum possible, you might like to read the fine print on your contract... 
any unauthorised "engineering" means the equipment can be re-possessed by 
the vendor immediately (if you are leasing it) and all warranties and 
service agreements are rendered null and void (if you own it.)

Furthermore, some of the upgrades require more than just brick removal. 
(addition of new buses, memory etc. to support the new capability.

Intel are just doing what everybody else does (but nobody talks about...)

Pete.
```

##### ↳ ↳ Re: Lets stop MF and MS bashing and start bashing Intel....

- **From:** HansJ <hans.igel@googlemail.com>
- **Date:** 2010-09-22T03:07:43-07:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<4d402daa-86b6-4419-9231-75bd741cec15@q18g2000vbm.googlegroups.com>`
- **References:** `<aeb46457-7819-4711-99a8-a59ddf74b46e@u13g2000vbo.googlegroups.com> <8fu0llFpa0U1@mid.individual.net>`

```
On 22 Sep., 11:30, "Pete Dashwood"
<dashw...@removethis.enternet.co.nz> wrote:
> Alistair wrote:
> > The following appeared on the BBC News web site:
…[58 more quoted lines elided]…
> "I used to write COBOL...now I can do anything."

Pete, this is a perfect description and there is nothing wrong with
it. The simple fact, that it is cheaper to build in the full
capability of a product and release the parts that the customer is
paying for just makes sense.

I can recall when I was a hardware engineer in the early 70's Univac
had a 9200 and a 9300 model. As far as I can recall, the processor
side of the upgrade - some features where not available to the 9200
model - was a jumper to be wired into the backpanel. For test
purposes, there was a switch and when the hardware engineer did forget
to set it back, the system was twice as fast.

The same would apply for systems based on micro code, a different
microcode would allow a completly different system with a completly
different pricing structure based on competitive situations. This was
true for the Unisys model 7E running the OS/3 OS and the same hardware
running MCP with a different microcode.
```

Howard,

I'd see it as being positive.

The product pricing will reflect whatever the "market price" would be,
for both, the limited and the full performance. So if the limited
perfomance product has a given price, this will most likely be in the
range of comparable products. So nothing wrong with it.

A similar situation may arise when you are looking in "overclocking" a
CPU. This is not much of a difference other than that you are taking
the risk of malefunctioning.

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Lets stop MF and MS bashing and start bashing Intel....

_(thread depth: 4)_

- **From:** "Pete Dashwood" <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz>
- **Date:** 2010-09-23T12:22:59+12:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<8fvkv5Fot5U1@mid.individual.net>`
- **References:** `<aeb46457-7819-4711-99a8-a59ddf74b46e@u13g2000vbo.googlegroups.com> <8fu0llFpa0U1@mid.individual.net> <e9486ee7-673d-4ef3-97c0-e1aad13c6c10@v23g2000vbi.googlegroups.com> <8fuoopFfu2U1@mid.individual.net>`

```
Bill Gunshannon wrote:
> In article
> <e9486ee7-673d-4ef3-97c0-e1aad13c6c10@v23g2000vbi.googlegroups.com>,
…[80 more quoted lines elided]…
> AMD sales in the near future.

Really?

AMD do it too... :-)

As do Motorola.

I tried to explain that this is standard industry practice and has been for 
decades.

With these chips (which are complex enough to emulate millions of 
transistors on a circut board) the 'brick' is in control software on the 
chip itself. Setting a certain property tells the control circuitry how to 
behave.

(I was lucky enough to get a private conducted tour through a Texas 
Instruments chip manufacturing facility in the U.K. during the '90s and this 
was explained to me at the time. They had hardware/software development 
facilities that were simply years ahead of the time There were large wall 
size visual screens that could select components and drag them to a 
solution, with the interfaces being recognised and automatically connected 
in. As a hardware company their software development was very much based on 
the principles used for the hardware design. It was pretty impressive.)

It is about containing production costs and having a product range to 
market.


Pete.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Lets stop MF and MS bashing and start bashing Intel....

_(thread depth: 5)_

- **From:** Richard <riplin@Azonic.co.nz>
- **Date:** 2010-09-22T19:15:06-07:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<b556bfd5-ff9f-44bb-ba24-9c0028e47587@y32g2000prc.googlegroups.com>`
- **References:** `<aeb46457-7819-4711-99a8-a59ddf74b46e@u13g2000vbo.googlegroups.com> <8fu0llFpa0U1@mid.individual.net> <e9486ee7-673d-4ef3-97c0-e1aad13c6c10@v23g2000vbi.googlegroups.com> <8fuoopFfu2U1@mid.individual.net> <8fvkv5Fot5U1@mid.individual.net>`

```
On Sep 23, 12:22 pm, "Pete Dashwood"
<dashw...@removethis.enternet.co.nz> wrote:
> Bill Gunshannon wrote:
> > In article
…[90 more quoted lines elided]…
> decades.

Industry standard practice has mostly been to test individual chips
and recover the best performance they are capable of. CPUs that fail
to run at full design speed are derated to lower MHz until they run
successfully. Dual or quad core chips where one has failed are sold a
1 or 3 core with the non-functional disabled.

Intel 80486SX were 80486 where the FPU failed to work and was
disabled. This could be 'upgraded' to a full 80486DX by adding a 80487
which was actually a 80486 with working FPU and the CPU blanked off.

Many of these downgraded chips can be overclocked to run at higher
speeds as long as sufficient cooling is added (and as long as the
manufacturer hasn't done something to prevent this).

Some more recent AMD processors cater for overclocking and there are
guidelines about doing this. Intel has previously put locks on the
ability to overclock.

Many of Itels processors are locked so they can't be overclocked,
which saves warranty replacements. To compete with AMD they have
'enthusiast' edition i3, i5 and i7 that can be overclocked in specific
motherboards that can cope with this, but they are more expensive.

This move, where an upgrade can be purchased and it is done by a code
unlocking the extra performance, seems to be a way of bypassing the
OEMs to collect more revenue directly. Instead of the OEM selling an
upgrade and only passing some of the revenue to Intel, it could be
that Intel gets all the $50 (or so).

So why would OEMs sell those systems ?

> transistors on a circut board) the 'brick' is in control software on the
…[18 more quoted lines elided]…
> "I used to write COBOL...now I can do anything."

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Lets stop MF and MS bashing and start bashing Intel....

_(thread depth: 6)_

- **From:** "Pete Dashwood" <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz>
- **Date:** 2010-09-23T22:27:54+12:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<8g0oddFj5bU1@mid.individual.net>`
- **References:** `<aeb46457-7819-4711-99a8-a59ddf74b46e@u13g2000vbo.googlegroups.com> <8fu0llFpa0U1@mid.individual.net> <e9486ee7-673d-4ef3-97c0-e1aad13c6c10@v23g2000vbi.googlegroups.com> <8fuoopFfu2U1@mid.individual.net> <8fvkv5Fot5U1@mid.individual.net> <b556bfd5-ff9f-44bb-ba24-9c0028e47587@y32g2000prc.googlegroups.com>`

```
Richard wrote:
> On Sep 23, 12:22 pm, "Pete Dashwood"
> <dashw...@removethis.enternet.co.nz> wrote:
…[96 more quoted lines elided]…
> and recover the best performance they are capable of.

That may well be the practice now, but it wasn't always. TI simply discarded 
chips that were not perfect. Chips were printed to something like a slice of 
silicon salami that might have 50 complete chips on it. The chips were 
tested in the laboratory by specially built machines and flawed ones were 
simply "erased". A laser device then cut the chips that were not "erased". 
They used to lose around  8 -15% of the run. At the time I was there they 
were looking for ways to improve this figure and had already developed ways 
of scanning the 'salami' roll so that impurities were pushed out of it, 
before it was sliced and printed. Flawed chips were accepted as a cost of 
production and eliminated.

It may well be that with the complexity of modern multi-core chips they can 
recycle less than perfect chips as lower performance ones.

The principle of using a single production run and then progressively 
inhibiting it to create a marketing range is the point I was making. The 
'mechanics' of how they do this probably varies widely with modern chips.

>CPUs that fail
> to run at full design speed are derated to lower MHz until they run
> successfully.

That is certainly better than discarding them, I guess.


> Dual or quad core chips where one has failed are sold a
> 1 or 3 core with the non-functional disabled.
…[11 more quoted lines elided]…
> ability to overclock.

Overclocking has been usual practice for some time amongst gamers wanting to 
extract the last ounce of performance. Manufacturers have always frowned on 
it.
>
> Many of Itels processors are locked so they can't be overclocked,
…[10 more quoted lines elided]…
> So why would OEMs sell those systems ?

Perhaps because people want Intel Chips?

My own experience with both Intel and AMD has been that they both provide 
excellent products (I've never had a problem with either of them, but I've 
never pushed the chips, either.)

Pete.
```

```
On Thu, 23 Sep 2010 11:45:20 -0700 (PDT), Richard
<riplin@Azonic.co.nz> wrote:

>> It may well be that with the complexity of modern multi-core chips they can
>> recycle less than perfect chips as lower performance ones.
>
>Manufacturers have been selling less than perfect chips as lower
>performance for decades.

Depending on why the chip is less than perfect.


>> > So why would OEMs sell those systems ?
>>
…[16 more quoted lines elided]…
>*Intel manages to keep AMD out of the major OEMs such as Dell.

How do they do that?   By producing demand for their product.  

There is *lots* of software that is priced by how much it is used. How
much does a payroll package cost?   It depends on how big of a payroll
is being processed.    The big company's payroll package didn't cost
more to make than the small company's payroll package.   

And movie makers don't charge the same amount to rent a movie for a
day at each theater.   They charge by the amount of tickets the
theater sells.    And they periodically hire people to verify the
counts that the theater states.



What vendors have found is that an effective way to sell software at
different prices is to offer "discounts" to small buyers.   I expect
Intel to use the same psychology.   The "crippled" chip will sell at a
cheaper cost than the upgraded chip.    That will make more
prospective customers happy than stating that the "fixed" chip costs
more.

But the difference is only in marketing, not in reality.
```

```
On Sep 24, 7:21 am, Howard Brazee <how...@brazee.net> wrote:
> On Thu, 23 Sep 2010 11:45:20 -0700 (PDT), Richard
>
…[30 more quoted lines elided]…
> How do they do that?   By producing demand for their product.  

How naive of you:

http://www.networkworld.com/news/2010/060710-tech-argument-amd-intel.html

"""The Federal Trade Commission filed a formal antitrust lawsuit
against Intel in December 2009, just one month after the settlement,
alleging that Intel used illegal tactics such as threatening to
increase prices, end technology collaborations and shut off supply and
marketing support to manufacturers that purchased too many products
from Intel's competitors.

Intel also faces an antitrust lawsuit filed by New York Attorney
General Andrew Cuomo in November 2009, and it was assessed a $1.44
billion fine by European regulators in May 2009."""

see also:

http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/AMD_v._Intel

> There is *lots* of software that is priced by how much it is used. How
> much does a payroll package cost?   It depends on how big of a payroll
…[13 more quoted lines elided]…
> more.

I don't have a problem with scaled sales.

The problem that I see is where, for example, an agent sells a cheap
or limited version of some software (or in this case an OEM and
hardware) at a discount after doing all the sales work and then is cut
out of the upgrade revenue and profit because the customer gets the
upgrade directly from the source.

Whether this is good for the customer or the manufacturer is not the
point, it is the agent or OEM that is being screwed. They are channel
between the two and on the one hand are controlled by the supplier
wielding monopoly power and on the other have to compete on price with
other agents or OEMs.

In your theatre example it is like the film company gets a cut of all
the sales at the cheapest seat rate but gets to keep _all_ the
additional price of (upgrade to) more expensive seats. And if the
theatre doesn't agree then they won't get any films at all.
```

```
Howard Brazee wrote:
> On Fri, 24 Sep 2010 12:47:34 +1200, "Pete Dashwood"
> <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz> wrote:
…[11 more quoted lines elided]…
> Intel has forced Apple to buy Intel chips?

Short answer: I don't know.

Long answer: Given their shenanigans with Dell, we could expect that 
pressure was applied to Apple and, probably, to most consumers of Intel 
chips. In perspective, it is probable that their position as a leading chip 
maker gave them this kind of leverage and some people at Intel got a bit 
"carried away". I think the anti-trust suit has curbed some of this (I hope 
so... I wouldn't like to see Intel disappear altogether), and we should also 
bear in mind that their competitors do similar things, if they can. (AMD 
have exclusive deals (for certain types of device over a specific period) 
with several companies, including Acer, Lenovo and Hewlett Packard.)

It is a bit of a minefield. For example, let's say there is a shortage of 
chips (which there historically has been...) ... any chip manufacturer is 
going to look after their best customers at the expense of their lesser 
customers. This creates a market within a market. Small manufacturer needs 
chips and in effect is held to ransom and can only get them if they sign 
restrictive contracts and comply with vendors conditions. Is it shady? Or is 
it just business?

It is certainly not a business I would want to be in...

Pete..
```

with their customers, the picture isn't always quite so gloomy.

```
In article <8g565aFve3U1@mid.individual.net>,
Pete Dashwood <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz> wrote:
>docdwarf@panix.com wrote:
>> In article
…[29 more quoted lines elided]…
>sad reflection on the downside of human nature.

That view is not unique to you, Mr Dashwood, but I've found that view (and 
ones similar) to be another delightful inconsistency of Stuff That Humans 
Do.  For example... when the Strong take advantage of the Weak or a 
'clever loophole' is discovered that causes an otherwise equitable 
transaction to suddenly favor one party over the other a common response 
I've seen is a shrug and 'What do you expect... it's just Human Nature.'

On the other hand... when the opposite occurs, the Strong assist the Weak 
in a manner the Weak just could not do or a contractual impasse is worked 
out to the benefit of all involved I don't recall hearing 'What do you 
expect... it's just Human Nature.'

This, among other experiences, have lead me to the conclusion that I 
haven't met enough Humans to make any statements about their Nature beyond 
a simple 'Folks can act like that, aye.'

DD
```

you haven't met everybody, and some of them are capable of astonishing good 
things. Most people are doing the best they can. Nobody gets out of bed in 
the morning determined to screw up their own and everybody else's life."

On 24 Sep., 17:08, Howard Brazee <how...@brazee.net> wrote:
> On Fri, 24 Sep 2010 01:02:59 -0700 (PDT), HansJ
>
…[11 more quoted lines elided]…
> doing anything.   If they do look around they save money.

... sure, we have choices. But lets just use an example for an
insurance company. One of my daughters had a bad accident requiring
her to stay in the hospital for more than 6 weeks. I had an insurance
covering accidents for the family and when she moved out and got
married, I told her to get a similar insurance for herself what she
did. On the other hand I did forget to cancel her on my insurance
package. Her insurance requested all the facts and payed within a
fairly short time. My insurance that got the same facts from the same
hospital was playing nasty tricks trying to reduce their payment. It
took quite some effort and writing and more than a year to get this
sorted out. So the choice os obviously there to change the insurance -
which will be done. The liability insurance is playing the same game
trying to get people tired of fighting and agreeing to a small
ammount. This is not over after 2 years ans takes lawyers to sort this
out. So yes there is a choice, but sometines we need the proof that we
got screwed before choosing.

I did work on a large project a while ago where several big IT players
asked for our expertise. There was one company (typically winning many
of the projects in Germany's public sector) that had a "claim manager"
position within the project team. This claim manager was supposed to
find out where additional revenew could be generated during the
project. I did opt against them as they would have screwed us as well
as the customer.

Politics is another such arena where screwing others is part of the
business model...

> So whose fault is this?   We can blame the company if we want.   We
> also stop at the same restaurant or coffee house again and again. Did
> the coffee house "lock us in" by providing "good enough" coffee?
>
... this is your positive example of choice and we could still go for
the other coffee which is only served in black cups as you could see
the ground of the cup...

Regards Hans

HansJ wrote:
> On 23 Sep., 20:45, Richard <rip...@Azonic.co.nz> wrote:
>> On Sep 23, 10:27 pm, "Pete Dashwood"
…[23 more quoted lines elided]…
> as good as a contract.

Good for you, Hans.

I sealed a deal yesterday by giving my word to deliver. I will do so, and I 
trust the client to pay me because he said he would. There are people you 
can do this with, and there are people you cannot, but the fact that there 
are still some you can trust, means that all hope should not be abandoned.

For long complex deliveries, a written contract protects both parties, but 
for short term stuff, with clients who you know, sometimes it is quicker to 
simply shake hands and get on with it.

I have tried over the years (and still do) to make sure that people can 
trust me, and  I do what I say I'm going to do. It seems to work. I had an 
agency call me a couple of weeks ago offering a bad debt collection service. 
I was able to inform them that I have no need of such a service as we have 
nobody who has ever stiffed us or not paid up. (There have been a couple of 
clients who I was happy to talk to and work out a restructuring of their 
payments; in business sometimes unexpected cash flow problems can arise and 
that is nobody's fault, neither does it indicate an intent to defraud. I 
like to think our clients will do repeat business with us and will say nice 
things about us to others :-))

I also believe that people tend to treat you the way that you treat them.

Pete.

- **From:** "HeyBub" <heybub@NOSPAMgmail.com>
- **Date:** 2010-09-25T07:07:47-05:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<dLKdnakwcbyIewDRnZ2dnUVZ_h6dnZ2d@earthlink.com>`
- **References:** `<aeb46457-7819-4711-99a8-a59ddf74b46e@u13g2000vbo.googlegroups.com> <8fu0llFpa0U1@mid.individual.net> <e9486ee7-673d-4ef3-97c0-e1aad13c6c10@v23g2000vbi.googlegroups.com> <8fuoopFfu2U1@mid.individual.net> <8fvkv5Fot5U1@mid.individual.net> <b556bfd5-ff9f-44bb-ba24-9c0028e47587@y32g2000prc.googlegroups.com> <8g0oddFj5bU1@mid.individual.net> <2e668eb0-42da-413d-9494-79962bf0951a@i4g2000prf.googlegroups.com> <f1f6e629-5a46-4aef-b049-cb5747206726@u13g2000vbo.googlegroups.com> <8g55g3Fs9iU1@mid.individual.net>`

```
Pete Dashwood wrote:
>
> Good for you, Hans.
…[22 more quoted lines elided]…
> them.

A "contract" does not (usually) have to be written. Those that require 
memorialization can be remember by the acronym of "MY LEGS" (Marriage, more 
than one Year, Land, Executor of an Estate, Goods of high value, and 
Services of high value). But even then contracts are subject to the usual 
practice of the industry.

Some time back (1984), Pennzoil agreed to buy some Louisiana oil leases from 
the J. Paul Getty estates. Had a news conference, hand-shaking, flashbulbs, 
and everything.

The next day, Texaco called Getty and wanted to know if they had a "deal" 
with Pennzoil or a "real deal." Getty said they had a handshake and that the 
legal documents would take a couple of weeks to work out. Texaco then said 
"We'll give you more for your leases than you're getting from Pennzoil." 
Getty said "Okay, but you've got to indemnify us against any breach of 
contract suit by Pennzoil." Texaco agreed.

When the President of Pennzoil heard that his deal was canceled, he went 
nuts! "Texaco has been fuckin' people in the oil patch for fifty years! This 
shit stops now!"

(In the oil patch, a piece of property may change hands five times in one 
day and it may take months for the paper to catch up. Everything is done on 
a handshake.)

Long story short: Texaco lost. $10 billion lost.

An interesting sidebar, the U.S. Supreme Court, in an unprecedented action, 
met by telephone and unanimously ordered a federal judge in New York (?) to 
stay the hell away from the case.

I'm sure it helped that the president of Pennzoil, Hugh Liedtke, had a 
friend and former business partner, in high office. His friend was a war 
hero, former Congressman, Ambassador to the United Nations, Chairman of the 
Republican National Committee, First U.S. Representative to Red China, 
Director of the Council on Foreign Relations, Director of Central 
Intelligence, and, in 1984, Vice-President of the United States, thereby 
proving that one can come to Texas with just a few million in one's jeans 
and make good.
```

On 25 Sep., 04:35, "Pete Dashwood"
<dashw...@removethis.enternet.co.nz> wrote:
> HansJ wrote:
> > On 23 Sep., 20:45, Richard <rip...@Azonic.co.nz> wrote:
…[50 more quoted lines elided]…
> "I used to write COBOL...now I can do anything."

Yep, theses relationships still exist and I enjoy working in this way.
With large companies it is not possible and there are good reasons for
that.

A friend of mine told me an example from Japan. He worked out a
contract with a japanese company and everything seemed to be discussed
and written down. Then on the way to the airport the japanese company
presented him a new contract with a substancially lower margin. They
said, we will stick to our initial contract, but it will not be
extended, or you sign the new contract and we will do business for a
long time. The deal fell thru.

I have no clue if this is common practice, but he told me that this is
not unusual.

Regards  Hans
```

```
Howard Brazee wrote:
> On Sat, 25 Sep 2010 14:35:46 +1200, "Pete Dashwood"
> <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz> wrote:
…[5 more quoted lines elided]…
> universal.

Some time back, the Mathematic Section editor of Scientific American ran 
some user-inspired tests to find out the best tactic in game theory when you 
must continue to deal with another (as in diplomatic relations).

The hands-down winner was Tit-For-Tat. That is, start out trusting your 
counterpart, then repeat his comply/not reaction. For example, begin with a 
trust transaction then, if your opposite number screws you, you screw him at 
the next opportunity.

I, personally, follow the "Scorched Earth" tactic: Abuse me once and you 
will experience my retribution forever.

Another tactic is "Refuse To Play."

A real-life scenario: When the Iranians took over the U.S. Embassy, 
Tit-for-Tat would indicate we take over theirs. Our government chose Refuse 
To Play and broke diplomatic relations ('til this day).
```

### Another test request

- **From:** "Bill Klein" <wmklein@noreply.ix.netcom.com>

```
If you have access to a compiler (other than a Micro Focus one - which I 
have already tested) that support "ANS'85 conformance flagging" (e.g. 
Enterprise COBOL with FLAGSTD(H) ),  I am pretty certain that Fujitsu also 
has such a flagging facility.

I have another test program that I would like to know what compilers do 
with.

Can you download the program from:
  http://home.comcast.net/~wmklein/DOX/alph85.cbl

and try compiling it with ANS'85 flaggin turned on.  I am interested in what 
(if any) flagging (or error) messages the compile gets.  If you do NOT get 
any flagging messages or error messages, could you "uncomment" the last MOVE 
and display statements and see if this gets flagging or error messages.  (I 
think it will PROBABLY get error messages).  Please remember to "turn on" 
seeing informational and flagging messages - If your compiler doesn't 
default to doing this.

You can run the program, but I am not all that interested in the run-time 
results (at lest yet).

You can reply in this forum or to me off-line; whichever is easiest for you.
```

#### ↳ Re: Documenting COBOL system/screens

- **From:** Alistair <alistair@ld50macca.demon.co.uk>

## Thread: [OT] JCL-ish Difficulties - VSAM Index & Data not Associated w/ Cluster — 13 this month / 24 total

_Topics: [`Mainframe, z/OS, JCL, CICS`](topics.md#mainframe) · [`VSAM, files, sorting`](topics.md#files)_

### [OT] JCL-ish Difficulties - VSAM Index & Data not Associated w/ Cluster

- **From:** docdwarf@panix.com ()
- **Date:** 2010-09-29T14:00:22+00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<i7vgpm$cjv$1@reader1.panix.com>`

```

This one's kind of odd.  There's a naming convention on this site where a 
KSDS cluster must be named USERID.VSAM.NAME (or some reasonable facsimile 
thereof) and the index/data segments are USERID.VSAM.NAME.DATA and 
USERID.VSAM.NAME.INDEX.

The Friendly Folks in Ops - and I *always* try to keep on the good side of 
Ops - showed me something curious.  Using the ISPF Data Set List Utility 
and a wildcarded USERID.VSAM.NAM* showed nothing...

... but when the Dsname was left blank and volser AAAAA specified it 
showed both USERID.VSAM.NAME.DATA and USERID.VSAM.NAME.INDEX on vol AAAAA.

What is wanted is to delete both of them and I've not been able to.  
IDCAMS gives a variety of NOT FOUND messages for DELETE, IEFBR14 comes 
back with a 0 Return Code for (MOD,DELETE,DELETE) and the names still 
show... I even ran an IEFBR14 with (NEW,KEEP,KEEP) for the datasets and 
got the predictable 'duplicate name' error.

So... for creating they exist, for deleting they do not.  I believe that 
somewhere, somehow their cluster USERID.VSAM.NAME got blown away and the 
segments still retain some kind of addressing-dependency on something that 
no longer exists... and poring over the IDCAMS manual doesn't readily 
yield an answer as to how one might associate another, entirely different 
index/data segment with a new cluster.

Outside of 'reformat the entire pack and then restore everything *except* 
those two datasets'... any suggestions on how to get these hence?

Thanks much.

DD
```

Depending on RACF settings, you may need to put the file name in
quotes.  In some cases, RACF has a nasty habit (ok..it is by design)
of appending  your userid to the front of your file names.   So try an
ISPF 3.4 and then 'USERID.VSAM.NAME.*'  and see what you get.

>... but when the Dsname was left blank and volser AAAAA specified it 
>showed both USERID.VSAM.NAME.DATA and USERID.VSAM.NAME.INDEX on vol AAAAA.
>

This might indicate that the base cluster was renamed which is why you
can't find it any longer and the DATA and INDEX names are associated
with another base cluster.

>What is wanted is to delete both of them and I've not been able to.  
>IDCAMS gives a variety of NOT FOUND messages for DELETE, IEFBR14 comes 
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
>

This too would seem to indicate the base cluster was renamed.

>So... for creating they exist, for deleting they do not.  I believe that 
>somewhere, somehow their cluster USERID.VSAM.NAME got blown away and the 
…[10 more quoted lines elided]…
>DD

Do a LISTCAT on either the DATA or INDEX name.  It will show you what
base cluster they are attached to.

Regards,
```

Using the syntax LISTCAT ENTRY(USERID.VSAM.NAME.DATA) or LISTCAT 
ENTRY(USERID.VSAM.NAME.INDEX) gives the ever-popular:

IDC3012I ENTRY USERID.VSAM.NAME.DATA NOT FOUND                      
IDC3009I ** VSAM CATALOG RETURN CODE IS 8 - REASON CODE IS IGG0CLEG-42 
IDC1566I ** USERID.VSAM.NAME.DATA NOT LISTED                        

... and an IGG0CLEG-42 (pronounce IG-oh-klehg) for the .INDEX, as well.

Thanks much.

```
On Wed, 29 Sep 2010 14:00:22 +0000 (UTC), docdwarf@panix.com () wrote:

>
>This one's kind of odd.  There's a naming convention on this site where a 
…[9 more quoted lines elided]…
>showed both USERID.VSAM.NAME.DATA and USERID.VSAM.NAME.INDEX on vol AAAAA.

It looks like the cluster got deleted without the components being
deleted.  This has been discussed on bit.listserv.ibm-main (the mirror
of the listserv hosted by the University of Alabama).  I suggest
passing this on to the storage administrator (or systems programmer)
at your shop since the fix may require special security clearance in
RACF/Top Secret/ACF2/Other Security package.  The fix was convoluted
enough that I don't remember the details.  There may be something in
the DF/SMS series of manuals including managing catalogs.

Clark Morris

#### ↳ Re: [OT] JCL-ish Difficulties - VSAM Index & Data not Associated w/ Cluster

- **From:** Arnold Trembley <arnold.trembley@att.net>
- **Date:** 2010-09-30T02:59:10-05:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<DdCdnf3LAdXT3jnRnZ2dnUVZ5s2dnZ2d@giganews.com>`
- **In-Reply-To:** `<i7vgpm$cjv$1@reader1.panix.com>`
- **References:** `<i7vgpm$cjv$1@reader1.panix.com>`

```
On 9/29/2010 9:00 AM, docdwarf@panix.com wrote:
> This one's kind of odd.  There's a naming convention on this site where a
> KSDS cluster must be named USERID.VSAM.NAME (or some reasonable facsimile
…[29 more quoted lines elided]…
>

Doc, I've never had to do this, but I've seen similar problems discussed 
in bit.listserv.ibm-main.  So I did a bit of googling.

Here's something that might be worth a try:

http://www.lascon.co.uk/d002005.htm

***begin quote***

To delete a VSAM file, you just delete the cluster entry, and all the 
components are deleted with it. You can either do this with an IDCAMS 
DELETE job, or simply type DEL against the VSAM cluster name on a 3.4 
listing (this is explained in the Z/OS utility section)

If you have VSAM components without a cluster, then you can catalog them 
up using an IDCAMS RECATALOG command as below. You need to know the 
volumes the the uncatalogued components are held on

    DEFINE CLUSTER -
    (NAME(PIN.MVSOLZAX.ZMG)  -
     RECATALOG) -
   DATA (NAME(PIN.MVSOLZAX.ZMG.DATA) VOLUMES(DV000E) ) -
  INDEX (NAME(PIN.MVSOLZAX.ZMG.INDEX) VOLUMES(DV003B) )

Alternatively you can delete the uncatalogued components using IDCAMS 
statements like

//DD1       DD  VOL=SER=DV000E,DISP=SHR,UNIT=DISK
//DD2       DD  VOL=SER=DV003B,DISP=SHR,UNIT=DISK
//SYSIN   DD  *
  DELETE PIN.MVSOLZAX.ZMG.DATA -
       VVR FILE(DD1) -
     CATALOG(CATALOG.ICFUSER.USER4)
  DELETE PIN.MVSOLZAX.ZMG.INDEX -
       VVR FILE(DD2) -
     CATALOG(CATALOG.ICFUSER.USER4)

The TRUENAME is a Catalog record that relates a VSAM component to its 
cluster. Sometimes, usually after a failed attempt to delete a VSAM 
file, you can be left with an orphaned truename record in the catalog. 
If this happens you cannot recreate the file, you will get duplicate 
name failures if you try. The answer is to use IDCAMS DELETE with the 
TRUENAME parameter as below.

  DELETE PIN.MVSOLZAX.ZMG.DATA -
  TRUENAME

***end quote***

I'm not sure how you determine the catalog name, and I don't know if 
DELETE VVR requires additional security permissions that you might not have.

Good luck!
```
